# QA Results for quiz-5-padded-final.md

## Q01

### Grounding

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available.

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the severe threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of reasonable and ordinary firmness would have yielded to the exact same situational pressure to protect the unhoused.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower statutory threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of clear outweighing.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of the competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils rather than coercion by a human actor. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency itself. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Necessity, Ch 21, Refinement `necessity-proportionality-standard` (and `justification-vs-excuse`, `legislative-preemption-absolute`). Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available.

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the severe threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of reasonable and ordinary firmness would have yielded to the exact same situational pressure to protect the unhoused.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower statutory threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of clear outweighing.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of the competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. ### Edge Case Audit

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available.

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the severe threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of reasonable and ordinary firmness would have yielded to the exact same situational pressure to protect the unhoused.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower statutory threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of clear outweighing.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of the competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils rather than coercion by a human actor. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency itself. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available.

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the severe threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of reasonable and ordinary firmness would have yielded to the exact same situational pressure to protect the unhoused.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower statutory threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of clear outweighing.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of the competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils rather than coercion by a human actor. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency itself. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies necessity as the appropriate defense, as it involves a choice of evils brought about by natural forces (freezing weather) rather than human coercion. The option perfectly maps the strict common law elements—imminence, no alternatives, and the harm avoided clearly outweighing the harm caused—onto the facts. Given that Foot saved twelve people from freezing by occupying a condemned building, this provides the strongest legally grounded defense and clearly meets the required standard.
(b) Argument-for: While duress typically involves human coercion, a student might argue that the severe situational pressure of freezing weather functionally compelled Foot's actions just as a human threat would. The option correctly incorporates the "person of reasonable and ordinary firmness" standard, which is the objective measure used in excuse defenses. If a student believes the jurisdiction recognizes situational duress or blurs the line between necessity and duress in extreme emergencies, this would appear to be a compelling analysis.
(c) Argument-for: The Model Penal Code's formulation of necessity is famous for being more lenient than the common law, requiring only that the harm avoided be greater than the harm caused and abandoning the strict imminence requirement. A student could argue that framing Foot's defense under the MPC's lower threshold provides the conceptually "strongest" possible legal argument. Since saving twelve lives easily satisfies the MPC balancing test, this option looks like an attractive way to secure the defense.
(d) Argument-for: The common law strictly bars the necessity defense if the defendant is at fault in creating the emergency. A student could argue that by operating a shelter in a building that deteriorated to the point of condemnation, Foot brought the situation upon herself. Therefore, her prior operation of the deteriorating building constitutes fault that triggers the common law bar and completely precludes the necessity defense.
(e) Argument-for: The necessity defense is generally unavailable when the legislature has already weighed the competing values and made a deliberate choice. A student could strongly argue that the enactment of building safety codes and formal condemnation procedures reflects a definitive legislative judgment that condemned buildings are unsafe for habitation. Thus, these statutory frameworks categorically preempt any ad-hoc judicial balancing of harms.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is the only legally and factually correct analysis, perfectly applying the common law necessity elements to a natural emergency. Option (b) fails because duress strictly requires a threat of force from another human, not a natural condition like cold weather. Option (c) is factually incorrect because Fact 6 stipulates a common law jurisdiction, rendering the MPC standard inapplicable to this specific question. Option (d) contains a false legal claim that lawful prior operation of a building "automatically" constitutes culpable fault for a subsequent natural weather emergency. Option (e) falsely claims that "general" building codes "categorically" preempt necessity, whereas preemption requires a specific, particularized legislative intent to foreclose the defense in the precise emergency at issue.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "Foot has a strong duress defense because the severe threat of freezing to death compelled her" — wrong because common law duress requires coercion by a human actor, not natural weather conditions.
- (c): "Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC" — wrong because Fact 6 explicitly places the problem in a common law jurisdiction, making the MPC standard factually inapplicable.
- (d): "she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter" — wrong because prior lawful operation of a building does not automatically equate to culpable fault in creating a subsequent, independent weather emergency.
- (e): "the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense" — wrong because general safety codes do not constitute the specific, particularized legislative choice required to completely foreclose a necessity defense in an ad-hoc emergency.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q1.** Your supervisor asks you to evaluate Foot's strongest defense for her continued operation of the shelter in violation of the condemnation order (Facts 1 and 6). Which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Foot has a strong necessity defense because the harm avoided (twelve people exposed to life-threatening cold) clearly outweighs the harm caused (occupying a condemned building), the threat was imminent, and no alternative shelter beds were available.

(b) Foot has a strong duress defense because the severe threat of freezing to death compelled her to continue operating the shelter, and a person of reasonable and ordinary firmness would have yielded to the exact same situational pressure to protect the unhoused.

(c) Foot has a strong necessity defense because under the MPC, the harm avoided need only be "greater than" the harm caused, and protecting twelve lives easily satisfies that lower statutory threshold compared to the strict common law requirement of clear outweighing.

(d) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because she is automatically deemed at fault for creating the emergency solely by operating a shelter in a building that was later condemned, and the common law bars the defense when the defendant is at fault.

(e) Foot does not have a viable necessity defense because the mere existence of general building safety codes and condemnation procedures categorically preempts the necessity defense by foreclosing any judicial balancing of the competing harms in this specific case.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Necessity is the correct defense here because Foot faces a choice of evils rather than coercion by a human actor. Under the common law (Fact 6), a defendant must show: (1) the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) no reasonable legal alternative exists, and (4) the defendant was not at fault in creating the emergency. These elements are satisfied here. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not a natural condition like freezing weather. Option (c) is incorrect because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows the common law for necessity, not the MPC. Option (d) is incorrect because lawfully operating a shelter prior to its condemnation does not automatically constitute culpable fault in creating the weather emergency itself. Option (e) is incorrect because general building codes are not the type of particularized, specific legislative choice required to preempt the necessity defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, necessity-proportionality, justification-vs-excuse], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse necessity (choice of evils) with duress (human coercion), or apply MPC standards in a CL jurisdiction

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Doctrinal Framework Part I (necessity elements); justification-vs-excuse refinement; necessity-proportionality-standard refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under common law necessity, a defendant must establish that the harm avoided clearly outweighs the harm caused, the threat is imminent, and no reasonable legal alternative exists. Here, twelve people freezing to death constitutes a grave, imminent threat that indisputably outweighs the regulatory harm of occupying a condemned building. With no alternative shelter beds available, all elements of the common law necessity defense are perfectly satisfied, making this the most accurate analysis.
(b) Argument-for: Some modern theorists and students often conflate duress and necessity under the umbrella of "choice of evils." A student could argue that the extreme weather exerted unbearable pressure on Foot's free will, compelling her to act. Because a person of reasonable and ordinary firmness would also break the law to save twelve freezing people, the core rationale of excuse defenses is met, making duress a plausible defense to evaluate.
(c) Argument-for: The Model Penal Code deliberately relaxes the strict common law necessity standard, requiring only that the harm avoided be "greater than" the harm caused. A student might argue that the MPC standard provides Foot's strongest defense, as saving lives easily meets this lower statutory threshold without needing to prove the harm "clearly outweighs" the crime. If the student assumes the jurisdiction leans modern, this represents the most foolproof path to acquittal.
(d) Argument-for: Common law necessity strictly bars the defense if the defendant is at fault for creating the emergency. A student could argue that by choosing to operate a shelter in a building that was ultimately condemned, Foot set the factual chain of events in motion. Because she is the "but-for" cause of the shelter being located in an unsafe structure during the storm, she is at fault for the emergency, legally invalidating the necessity defense entirely.
(e) Argument-for: The necessity defense is completely unavailable if the legislature has already weighed the competing values and made a choice. A student could assert that municipal building codes and condemnation procedures represent a categorical legislative judgment that a condemned building is intolerably unsafe. Thus, the mere existence of these codes preempts any ad-hoc judicial balancing by a court, rendering the necessity defense categorically unviable.

Head-to-head: 
Option (a) correctly applies the common law elements of necessity to the facts. Option (b) is incorrect because duress requires coercion by another human being (a threat of unlawful force), not a natural threat like weather. Option (c) relies on the MPC standard, which contradicts Fact 6's stipulation of common law. Option (d) incorrectly applies the fault element by equating prior lawful operation of a building with culpable creation of the specific weather emergency, locked by the phrase "automatically deemed at fault... solely by". Option (e) incorrectly states that general building codes "categorically preempt" the defense; preemption requires a specific legislative intent to foreclose the choice of evils in the particular situation, not just general safety regulations.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "duress defense because the severe threat of freezing to death compelled her" — wrong because duress requires a threat of force from another person, not environmental or natural pressures.
- (c): "because under the MPC... statutory threshold" — wrong because Fact 6 specifies the jurisdiction follows common law, making MPC application legally incorrect here.
- (d): "automatically deemed at fault... solely by operating a shelter" — wrong because lawfully operating a building does not constitute culpable fault in creating a sudden weather emergency.
- (e): "mere existence of general building safety codes... categorically preempts" — wrong because general codes do not constitute the specific, particularized legislative preemption required to bar the necessity defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q02

### Grounding

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense against the condemnation violation charge has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Under common law doctrine, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense because necessity is classified as a personal excuse, meaning each individual volunteer must independently establish their own excuse defense to avoid criminal liability.
(b) If Foot establishes a successful necessity defense, her act is legally justified rather than merely excused; therefore, the volunteers who assisted in that act share in the justification and avoid accomplice liability.
(c) The volunteers face accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because common law accomplice liability attaches upon purposeful assistance in the prohibited conduct, precluding the transfer of any principal's defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification requires every participant to independently prove they faced a choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will share in the defense because common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses automatically transfer to accomplices who act with the same purpose as the principal.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Necessity is a justification defense (a "choice of evils"), meaning the defendant's conduct is deemed legally permissible under the circumstances. Because justifications attach to the act itself rather than the actor, an accomplice who assists in a justified act commits no crime and derives the benefit of the justification. Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. Option (d) is incorrect because accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the primary justification's elements to avoid derivative liability. Option (e) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not an excuse, and common law excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Derivative liability (justification vs. excuse) & Necessity as justification, Ch 18 & 21, Refinement `dl-justification-vs-excuse` and `justification-vs-excuse`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

(c) The volunteers face accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because common law accomplice liability attaches upon purposeful assistance in the prohibited conduct, precluding the transfer of any principal's defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification requires every participant to independently prove they faced a choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will share in the defense because common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses automatically transfer to accomplices who act with the same purpose as the principal.

**Answer:** (b)

Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. ### Edge Case Audit

(a) The volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense because necessity is classified as a personal excuse, meaning each individual volunteer must independently establish their own excuse defense to avoid criminal liability.
(b) If Foot establishes a successful necessity defense, her act is legally justified rather than merely excused; therefore, the volunteers who assisted in that act share in the justification and avoid accomplice liability.
(c) The volunteers face accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because common law accomplice liability attaches upon purposeful assistance in the prohibited conduct, precluding the transfer of any principal's defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification requires every participant to independently prove they faced a choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will share in the defense because common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses automatically transfer to accomplices who act with the same purpose as the principal.

**Answer:** (b)

Because justifications attach to the act itself rather than the actor, an accomplice who assists in a justified act commits no crime and derives the benefit of the justification. Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. Option (d) is incorrect because accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the primary justification's elements to avoid derivative liability. ### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense against the condemnation violation charge has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Under common law doctrine, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense because necessity is classified as a personal excuse, meaning each individual volunteer must independently establish their own excuse defense to avoid criminal liability.
(b) If Foot establishes a successful necessity defense, her act is legally justified rather than merely excused; therefore, the volunteers who assisted in that act share in the justification and avoid accomplice liability.
(c) The volunteers face accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because common law accomplice liability attaches upon purposeful assistance in the prohibited conduct, precluding the transfer of any principal's defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification requires every participant to independently prove they faced a choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will share in the defense because common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses automatically transfer to accomplices who act with the same purpose as the principal.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Necessity is a justification defense (a "choice of evils"), meaning the defendant's conduct is deemed legally permissible under the circumstances. Because justifications attach to the act itself rather than the actor, an accomplice who assists in a justified act commits no crime and derives the benefit of the justification. Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. Option (d) is incorrect because accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the primary justification's elements to avoid derivative liability. Option (e) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not an excuse, and common law excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue for (a) by drawing parallels to duress, which is classically an excuse. Since necessity involves being forced to act by overwhelming circumstances (much like duress involves being forced by a person), a student could reasonably deduce that necessity operates as a personal excuse. Under the rule that excuses are personal to the actor, the volunteers would not derive any protection from Foot's defense and would have to independently establish their own excuse.
(b) Argument-for: The core common law doctrine classifying defenses treats necessity as a justification ("choice of evils"). A justification defense means the act itself is considered legally permissible or right. Because accomplice liability requires assisting a wrongful criminal act, assisting a justified (and therefore lawful) act cannot result in accomplice liability. Thus, the volunteers derive complete protection from Foot's justification.
(c) Argument-for: A student might choose (c) by focusing rigidly on the *prima facie* elements of accomplice liability: intent to assist and actual assistance. If the volunteers purposefully aided the prohibited conduct, their liability elements are technically met. The argument would assert that common law accomplice liability evaluates the accomplice independently, categorically precluding the principal's affirmative defenses from transferring.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue for (d) by confusing derivative liability with the requirements of establishing a primary defense. They might believe that while a justification might exist, each individual claiming the protection must independently satisfy the elements of necessity. This posits that justification is not a blanket shield for the act, but a defense that must be individually proven by every participant.
(e) Argument-for: A student might select (e) by combining two misconceptions: categorizing necessity as an excuse (like duress) and believing that shared purpose enables the transfer of personal excuses. If the volunteers acted with the exact same purpose as Foot, the student might reason that agency principles allow them to step into Foot's shoes entirely, absorbing her excuse automatically.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly states the common law doctrine that necessity is a justification, which renders the underlying act lawful and shields accomplices from derivative liability. Option (a) fails because it explicitly misclassifies necessity as a personal excuse rather than a justification. Option (c) fails because it asserts an absolute bar on the transfer of "any" principal's defense, ignoring the fundamental rule that justifications defeat derivative liability by rendering the primary act lawful. Option (d) fails because it invents a requirement that derivative justification requires independent proof of the defense elements by every participant. Option (e) fails by misclassifying necessity as an excuse and explicitly stating the false claim that personal excuses automatically transfer to accomplices.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is classified as a personal excuse" — wrong because under common law, necessity is classified as a justification defense, not an excuse.
- (c): "precluding the transfer of any principal's defense" — wrong because justification defenses negate the wrongfulness of the act itself, thereby legally "transferring" to and protecting accomplices.
- (d): "derivative justification requires every participant to independently prove they faced a choice of evils" — wrong because an accomplice derives protection from the principal's justified act precisely because the act is rendered lawful, requiring no independent proof of the justification's elements.
- (e): "treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses automatically transfer" — wrong because necessity is a justification, and common law explicitly holds that personal excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q2.** Your supervisor asks whether Foot's necessity defense against the condemnation violation charge has any impact on the potential liability of volunteers who helped Foot keep the shelter open during the standoff (Facts 1 and 6). Under common law doctrine, which of the following best explains the relationship between Foot's defense and the volunteers' liability?

(a) The volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense because necessity is classified as a personal excuse, meaning each individual volunteer must independently establish their own excuse defense to avoid criminal liability.
(b) If Foot establishes a successful necessity defense, her act is legally justified rather than merely excused; therefore, the volunteers who assisted in that act share in the justification and avoid accomplice liability.
(c) The volunteers face accomplice liability regardless of Foot's defense because common law accomplice liability attaches upon purposeful assistance in the prohibited conduct, precluding the transfer of any principal's defense.
(d) The volunteers share in Foot's defense only if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity, because derivative justification requires every participant to independently prove they faced a choice of evils.
(e) The volunteers will share in the defense because common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses automatically transfer to accomplices who act with the same purpose as the principal.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Necessity is a justification defense (a "choice of evils"), meaning the defendant's conduct is deemed legally permissible under the circumstances. Because justifications attach to the act itself rather than the actor, an accomplice who assists in a justified act commits no crime and derives the benefit of the justification. Option (a) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not a personal excuse. Option (c) is incorrect because accomplice liability requires a legally wrongful act by the principal; if the act is justified, there is no crime to aid. Option (d) is incorrect because accomplices to a justified act do not need to independently satisfy the primary justification's elements to avoid derivative liability. Option (e) is incorrect because necessity is a justification, not an excuse, and common law excuses do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

**Tags:** chapters: [18, 21], topics: [justification-vs-excuse, necessity, derivative-liability], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students classify necessity as an excuse and deny volunteers a derivative defense

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, justification-vs-excuse refinement; Chapter 18, dl-justification-vs-excuse.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under common law doctrine, defenses are divided into justifications and excuses. A student could argue that necessity involves external circumstances that overpower the actor's will, similar to duress, thereby making it a personal excuse. If necessity is an excuse, it attaches only to the individual actor and does not transfer to accomplices, meaning the volunteers cannot share in Foot's defense and must independently establish their own.
(b) Argument-for: Necessity is universally classified as a justification under common law, specifically a "choice of evils," meaning the act itself is deemed socially beneficial or legally permissible. Because accomplice liability is derivative, it requires the principal to have committed a wrongful act. If Foot's act is justified, there is no criminal act to which the volunteers can be accomplices, allowing them to share in the justification and avoid liability.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that accomplice liability attaches the moment one purposely assists in prohibited conduct, focusing entirely on the accomplice's individual mens rea and actus reus. Under this strict individual-liability theory, a principal's defense—whether justification or excuse—is entirely personal and irrelevant to the accomplice. Thus, the volunteers face liability regardless of Foot's defense because accomplice liability strictly precludes the transfer of any defense from the principal.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that while necessity is a justification, criminal defenses are inherently individual. Therefore, derivative justification does not mean the accomplice gets a "free ride" on the principal's specific circumstances. Instead, the volunteers can only share in the defense if each independently satisfies the elements of necessity by proving they personally faced a choice of evils and chose the lesser harm.
(e) Argument-for: A student could conceptualize necessity as a "lesser-evils excuse" that mitigates blame rather than justifying the act entirely. Furthermore, under a joint-enterprise or shared-purpose theory, one could argue that all personal excuses automatically transfer to accomplices who act with the exact same purpose as the principal. Under this logic, the volunteers share in the defense because they shared Foot's purpose.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly applies the common law distinction between justifications and excuses: necessity is a justification that renders the act lawful, thus shielding accomplices from derivative liability. The distractors fail by explicitly misclassifying necessity as an excuse (a, e), misstating the rules of derivative liability by claiming justifications never transfer (c), or falsely claiming that derivative justification requires independent proof of the defense's elements by the accomplice (d). Every distractor contains at least one explicit, falsifiable misstatement of common law doctrine, locking them in as incorrect.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "necessity is classified as a personal excuse" — wrong because necessity is categorically classified as a justification under common law.
- (c): "precluding the transfer of any principal's defense" — wrong because common law derivative liability rules dictate that justification defenses (which render the act lawful) do transfer to accomplices.
- (d): "derivative justification requires every participant to independently prove they faced a choice of evils" — wrong because the nature of derivative justification is that the accomplice derives protection directly from the principal's justified act without needing to independently satisfy the defense's elements.
- (e): "common law treats necessity as a lesser-evils excuse, and all personal excuses automatically transfer" — wrong because necessity is a justification, and excuses are personal and explicitly do not automatically transfer to accomplices.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q03

### Grounding

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Fact 3). Holmes claims self-defense. Under the common law standard of self-defense (as applied in *People v. Goetz*), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest, purely subjective belief in the need for protective force is legally sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law, regardless of whether a reasonable person would share that assessment in those exact circumstances.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation—even after hearing Foot's warning about forced removal—would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force when faced with a small, unarmed woman identifying herself as a process server.

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable, legally overriding the need to evaluate the peaceful actions of the actual individual he encountered.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force. The common law imposes a strict duty to retreat prior to any use of defensive force, whether deadly or non-deadly, meaning he was legally required to exhaust all safe avenues of escape before standing his ground.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character." Under the common law, anyone with a history of violent offenses is categorically barred from asserting an affirmative self-defense claim, regardless of the immediate circumstances they face.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary against an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed (*People v. Goetz*). Holmes's "situation" legally incorporates his knowledge of Foot's prior warning. However, a reasonable person who hears a warning about forced removal, then encounters a small, unarmed woman who verbally identifies herself as a process server, would not conclude that an imminent use of unlawful force is underway. Therefore, his belief was objectively unreasonable. Option (a) is incorrect because the common law requires objective reasonableness, not merely an honest (purely subjective) belief. Option (c) is incorrect because while prior threat information is incorporated into the circumstances, it does not "automatically" make a subsequent belief objectively reasonable. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law duty to retreat applies only before using deadly force, and Holmes used non-deadly force. Option (e) is incorrect because a prior assault conviction does not categorically bar a defendant from raising a self-defense claim.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; People v. Goetz.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — objective reasonableness standard and situational incorporation, Ch 22, Refinement `objective-reasonableness-standard` and `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q3.** Your supervisor turns to Holmes's liability for shoving Lisa Chen (Fact 3). Holmes claims self-defense. Under the common law standard of self-defense (as applied in *People v. Goetz*), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because he honestly believed Lisa was part of a forced-removal team, and an honest, purely subjective belief in the need for protective force is legally sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law, regardless of whether a reasonable person would share that assessment in those exact circumstances.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because a reasonable person in Holmes's situation—even after hearing Foot's warning about forced removal—would not have believed force was necessary to defend against an imminent use of unlawful force when faced with a small, unarmed woman identifying herself as a process server.

(c) Holmes has a valid self-defense claim because Foot's earlier statement that "the city is going to send people to drag us out" constitutes prior threat information that automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable, legally overriding the need to evaluate the peaceful actions of the actual individual he encountered.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim solely because he failed to retreat before using force. The common law imposes a strict duty to retreat prior to any use of defensive force, whether deadly or non-deadly, meaning he was legally required to exhaust all safe avenues of escape before standing his ground.

(e) Holmes does not have a valid self-defense claim because his prior conviction for assault makes him a "person of violent character." Under the common law, anyone with a history of violent offenses is categorically barred from asserting an affirmative self-defense claim, regardless of the immediate circumstances they face.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Self-defense requires an objectively reasonable belief that force was necessary against an imminent use of unlawful force. The standard asks what a reasonable person in the defendant's situation would have believed (*People v. Goetz*). Holmes's "situation" legally incorporates his knowledge of Foot's prior warning. However, a reasonable person who hears a warning about forced removal, then encounters a small, unarmed woman who verbally identifies herself as a process server, would not conclude that an imminent use of unlawful force is underway. Therefore, his belief was objectively unreasonable. Option (a) is incorrect because the common law requires objective reasonableness, not merely an honest (purely subjective) belief. Option (c) is incorrect because while prior threat information is incorporated into the circumstances, it does not "automatically" make a subsequent belief objectively reasonable. Option (d) is incorrect because the common law duty to retreat applies only before using deadly force, and Holmes used non-deadly force. Option (e) is incorrect because a prior assault conviction does not categorically bar a defendant from raising a self-defense claim.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, imminence], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students apply a purely subjective standard or assume any prior experience makes the belief reasonable

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, objective-reasonableness-standard refinement; People v. Goetz.

(a) Argument-for: A student could argue this option is correct if they misinterpret the Model Penal Code standard or early subjective interpretations of self-defense as the common law rule. Thus, they could argue that if Holmes genuinely believed he was in danger, the standard is met, as some older or minority rules focus heavily on honest belief.
(b) Argument-for: This is the keyed correct answer. The student would argue that under *Goetz*, self-defense requires both an honest and an objectively reasonable belief that unlawful force is imminent. By evaluating the facts—Holmes facing a small, unarmed process server who explicitly announced her peaceful purpose—a reasonable person in Holmes's shoes (even with the prior warning) would not believe defensive force was immediately necessary. This correctly applies the hybrid objective-subjective standard of the common law.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue this is correct by over-extending the holding in *Goetz* regarding how prior experiences inform reasonableness. Since *Goetz* allows prior threats or experiences (like past muggings) to inform the reasonable person standard, a student might argue that Foot's specific warning about "forced removal" creates a presumption of reasonableness for any subsequent fear. They could argue this prior warning was the overriding context that legally justifies Holmes's reaction, regardless of the individual's actual actions.
(d) Argument-for: A student might argue for this option by misremembering or over-applying the common law duty to retreat. Since the common law generally prefers de-escalation and the preservation of life, they could argue that a universal duty to retreat exists before any force is used. They might assume that the duty to retreat broadly covers all forms of defensive force, including non-deadly force, to prevent unnecessary batteries or altercations.

The distractors are effectively designed, as each one pivots on a strict, falsifiable misstatement of common law doctrine. Option (a) explicitly asserts that a "purely subjective belief" is legally sufficient. Option (c) falsely claims prior threats "automatically" override immediate facts. Option (d) explicitly misstates the duty to retreat as applying to "non-deadly" force. Option (e) incorrectly states a "categorical bar" on self-defense for anyone with a violent history. Every distractor is locked with absolute phrasing that makes it demonstrably incorrect under established law. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "an honest, purely subjective belief in the need for protective force is legally sufficient to establish self-defense under the common law, regardless of whether a reasonable person would share that assessment" — wrong because the common law (*Goetz*) explicitly requires objective reasonableness.
- (c): "automatically makes any subsequent belief in danger objectively reasonable, legally overriding the need to evaluate the peaceful actions of the actual individual" — wrong because prior threats merely inform the circumstances; they do not automatically override the objective assessment of immediate facts.
- (d): "strict duty to retreat prior to any use of defensive force, whether deadly or non-deadly" — wrong because the common law duty to retreat, where it exists, applies exclusively to the use of deadly force.
- (e): "anyone with a history of violent offenses is categorically barred from asserting an affirmative self-defense claim" — wrong because there is no such categorical bar preventing an individual with a criminal record from asserting self-defense against a new threat.

Recommended fix: none

## Q04

### Grounding

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members. Even though the threatened harm was aimed at his mother, the traditional rules strictly mandate that the defendant must be the direct target of the severe injury.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. Although the threat of severe harm targeted a close family member, the strict standard does not allow for a delay in the execution of the threatened physical injury.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons. While the threat to his mother involved serious bodily injury, the strict traditional rules bar the application of this affirmative excuse whenever the defendant commits a property offense, regardless of coercion.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard. Even though the harm was scheduled for a future date, his documented pattern of enforcing ultimatums dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-imminence-proximity`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members. Even though the threatened harm was aimed at his mother, the traditional rules strictly mandate that the defendant must be the direct target of the severe injury.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. Although the threat of severe harm targeted a close family member, the strict standard does not allow for a delay in the execution of the threatened physical injury.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons. While the threat to his mother involved serious bodily injury, the strict traditional rules bar the application of this affirmative excuse whenever the defendant commits a property offense, regardless of coercion.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard. Even though the harm was scheduled for a future date, his documented pattern of enforcing ultimatums dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q4.** Returning to this jurisdiction's default rules (Fact 6), your supervisor asks about Holmes's claim that he stole the $5,000 under duress (Fact 4). Under the common law, which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because Nietzsche threatened serious bodily injury to Holmes's mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's history, and the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline that places the threatened harm days in the future.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress requires a threat directed at the defendant personally, and the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members. Even though the threatened harm was aimed at his mother, the traditional rules strictly mandate that the defendant must be the direct target of the severe injury.

(c) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was not imminent -- Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, and the common law requires a present and immediate threat, not one that will materialize days later. Although the threat of severe harm targeted a close family member, the strict standard does not allow for a delay in the execution of the threatened physical injury.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law, which limits the defense to offenses against persons. While the threat to his mother involved serious bodily injury, the strict traditional rules bar the application of this affirmative excuse whenever the defendant commits a property offense, regardless of coercion.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense because a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific, and Nietzsche's history of violence makes the threat both credible and specific enough to satisfy the common law standard. Even though the harm was scheduled for a future date, his documented pattern of enforcing ultimatums dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the common law (Fact 6), duress requires (1) a threat of death or serious bodily injury, (2) the threat is imminent, (3) the threat targets the defendant or a close relation, and (4) no reasonable legal alternative exists. Nietzsche's threat to "hurt her bad" satisfies the threat threshold and the target requirement (Holmes's mother is a close relation). However, the threat lacks imminence under the common law's strict standard. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday -- multiple days away. The common law requires a "present and immediate" threat, not one that will materialize days later. Option (a) falsely claims that the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats. This conflates the strict common-law rule with the *Dingwall* relaxation; under the common law, imminence requires a present and immediate threat, and a pattern of past violence does not substitute for temporal proximity. Nietzsche gave Holmes until Friday, defeating the imminence element. Option (b) states that the common law "does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" -- the false proposition is this scope limitation. The common law extends duress to threats against the defendant or a close relation, and a mother is a close relation. Option (d) fabricates a crime-type limitation -- the false proposition is that "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes." The common law murder bar applies only to homicide; duress is available for property crimes. Option (e) states that "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" -- the false proposition is this restatement of the imminence rule. The common law requires imminence as a separate element; credibility and specificity do not substitute for temporal immediacy.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence, duress-threat-threshold, duress-target-scope], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and family relationship while overlooking that "until Friday" defeats CL imminence

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-threat-threshold refinement; duress-imminence-proximity refinement (CL requires imminent threat); Doctrinal Framework Part II.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the common law, a credible and severe threat of injury to a close relative can sometimes blur the lines of traditional imminence if the threat is inescapable. A student might argue that when a threatening party has a documented history of enforcing ultimatums, the inevitability of the harm transforms the future deadline into a functionally immediate threat. Option (a) appeals to this logic by explicitly stating that a documented pattern satisfies the imminence requirement, validating the defense.

(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that the strictest, most traditional version of the common law limits the duress defense to threats against the defendant alone. Because duress requires an immediate, overpowering physical threat that breaks the defendant's will, the law historically required the defendant to be the direct target of the physical harm. Option (b) captures this restrictive view, asserting that threats to third parties, even close family members, categorically cannot support the defense.

(c) Argument-for: Under the common law, the duress defense strictly requires a "present, imminent, and impending" threat of death or serious bodily injury. The threat here was conditioned on a future deadline ("until Friday"), meaning it was not immediate and Holmes had time to seek legal alternatives, such as contacting the police. While the threat targeted a close relative (his mother) and involved severe injury, the lack of strict temporal imminence is fatal. Option (c) correctly concludes that the delay in execution defeats the imminence requirement.

(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that duress is subject to significant categorical limitations under the common law, most notably the bar against using it for intentional homicide. One might erroneously extend this logic to argue that property offenses also lack the necessary physical harm to justify submitting to coercion. Option (d) presents a bright-line rule that duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes, suggesting the common law strictly limits the defense to offenses against persons.

(e) Argument-for: While the common law generally mentions imminence, a student might argue that the core purpose of the imminence requirement is simply to ensure the threat is genuine and unavoidable. If a threat is highly credible and specific—bolstered by a history of violence—the strict temporal requirement is conceptually satisfied. Option (e) relies on this functional interpretation, asserting that Nietzsche's documented pattern of violence dispenses with the need for immediate temporal proximity.

Head-to-head: 
Option (c) accurately applies the common law's strict imminence element, identifying that a threat to be carried out "until Friday" is not present and immediate. The distractors all rely on explicit misstatements of common law doctrine. Option (a) wrongly claims that a documented pattern mathematically satisfies imminence regardless of deadlines. Option (b) falsely asserts that the common law categorically excludes threats to close family members. Option (d) fabricates a categorical bar on duress for theft crimes, misapplying the homicide exception. Option (e) explicitly, and incorrectly, states that a threat need not be imminent under the common law if it is credible and specific. Since each distractor hinges on a locked, objectively false claim, the question is highly defensible.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's imminence requirement is satisfied whenever the threatening party has a documented pattern of carrying out threats, regardless of any deadline" — wrong because the common law strictly requires temporal immediacy; a pattern of past violence does not override a multi-day deadline.
- (b): "the common law does not extend the defense to threats against third parties, including family members" — wrong because the common law explicitly allows the defense for threats directed at the defendant or a close relation (like a mother).
- (d): "duress is categorically unavailable for theft crimes under the common law" — wrong because the absolute categorical bar applies only to intentional homicide, not to theft or other property offenses.
- (e): "a threat need not be imminent so long as it is credible and specific" — wrong because the common law strictly requires imminence as a distinct, mandatory element that cannot be waived by credibility or specificity.

Recommended fix: none

(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that under certain modern interpretations or exceptions (such as battered woman syndrome or continuous coercion theories), a documented pattern of past violence can constructively satisfy the imminence requirement. This distractor appeals to test-takers who mistakenly believe the strict common law has a "past pattern" exception that overrides future deadlines, conflating a subjective fear standard with the rigid objective temporal rules.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that the strictest, most historical formulations of the common law restricted the duress defense exclusively to threats against the defendant personally. An unconfident student might believe that extending the defense to family members or close relations is purely a Model Penal Code or modern statutory innovation, making (b) appear to correctly apply the traditional "strict mandate."

Because Nietzsche gave Holmes "until Friday" to obtain the money, the threat is delayed by multiple days, affording Holmes ample time to alert the police or pursue other legal alternatives. ## Q05

### Grounding

<!-- grounding: GROUNDING-FAIL -->

**Safety Block Triggered.**

Error: Empty string returned. Finish Reason: FinishReason.MALFORMED_FUNCTION_CALL. Raw prompt preview: You are a grounding verifier for a single criminal law exam question. Your sole authority is the cha...

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the *Dingwall* reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money from the shelter, that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable to protect his mother. Does the *Dingwall* approach allow Holmes to satisfy the imminence requirement for duress? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because *Dingwall* established that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the *Dingwall* reasoning relaxing the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former associates.
(c) Yes, because *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the *Dingwall* exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is committed.
(e) No, because the *Dingwall* exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or third party.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens *Dingwall* into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that duress under *Dingwall* cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

check 3: pass
check 4: pass
check 5: pass
