### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the *Dingwall* reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money from the shelter, that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable to protect his mother. Does the *Dingwall* approach allow Holmes to satisfy the imminence requirement for duress? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because *Dingwall* established that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the *Dingwall* reasoning relaxing the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former associates.
(c) Yes, because *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the *Dingwall* exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is committed.
(e) No, because the *Dingwall* exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or third party.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens *Dingwall* into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that duress under *Dingwall* cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that the underlying psychological rationale of *Dingwall*—that prolonged, unpredictable threats create a constant state of fear—should logically extend to severe extortion. The facts state Nietzsche had threatened Holmes for months and police intervention was futile, perfectly mimicking the learned helplessness of battering victims. Therefore, the student might conclude *Dingwall* established a broader rule that any sustained threat pattern negates the strict physical imminence requirement, extending to anyone facing severe, continuous threats.
(b) Argument-for: *Dingwall* explicitly grounded its relaxation of the imminence requirement in the widely recognized psychological dynamics of intimate partner battering and coercive control. Extending this tailored exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes would completely swallow the traditional strict imminence rule for the duress defense. Because Holmes and Nietzsche's dynamic is simply an ongoing extortion dispute between former associates, the *Dingwall* exception does not apply. Thus, Holmes cannot satisfy the imminence requirement here.
(c) Argument-for: A student might interpret *Dingwall* as a wholesale rejection of the imminence element in continuous threat scenarios. Given that Nietzsche has a track record of threatening Holmes over several months and the authorities are powerless, a credible threat of future harm clearly exists. The student could conclude that *Dingwall* entirely replaces strict temporal imminence with a mere requirement of a "credible threat of future harm" based on past severe violence, making Holmes eligible for the defense.
(d) Argument-for: A student could mistakenly believe that *Dingwall* only excuses the lack of immediate physical proximity when the defendant is functionally incapacitated or imprisoned by the coercer. Under this reading, the relaxed imminence standard compensates for the coercer's physical absence only if the defendant is actively confined and thus helpless. Since Holmes is freely moving about (enough to burglarize a shelter independently), he does not meet this strict confinement criterion, leading to a "No" outcome.
(e) Argument-for: A student might reason that the *Dingwall* framework, being built on the dynamics of domestic battering, is inherently focused on the trauma and physical danger directed at the battered individual themselves. Because Holmes is acting to protect his mother rather than himself, the student could argue that the *Dingwall* exception is strictly limited to self-directed threats. Therefore, the generalized threat to a third party would legally preclude Holmes from invoking the relaxed imminence standard.

Head-to-head: 
Option (b) is unequivocally correct because *Dingwall* explicitly tailored its relaxed imminence standard to the unique dynamics of battering and coercive control, consciously declining to extend it to generalized extortion disputes. Option (a) fails because it asserts the rule applies "regardless of the relationship," which contradicts the core premise of *Dingwall*. Option (c) incorrectly claims the imminence requirement is "eliminated entirely" rather than contextually modified. Option (d) incorrectly mandates physical confinement, directly contradicting the precise holding in *Dingwall* that allows the defense even when the defendant is unconfined and the coercer is absent. Option (e) fails by baselessly asserting *Dingwall* nullifies the traditional duress principle that threats against close family members can sustain the defense. All distractors feature hard, absolute, and falsifiable legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "regardless of the relationship between the coercer and the defendant" — wrong because *Dingwall* specifically limits its relaxed imminence standard to the specific dynamics of battering and coercive control, making the nature of the relationship determinative.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" — wrong because *Dingwall* modifies how imminence is evaluated (incorporating psychological proximity) rather than completely eliminating the legal requirement of an imminent threat.
- (d): "strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined" — wrong because *Dingwall* expressly held that a reasonable fear of imminent harm can exist even when the defendant is not physically confined or in the immediate presence of the coercer.
- (e): "strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because *Dingwall* does not abrogate the fundamental duress rule that threats of death or serious bodily injury directed at third parties (such as family members) are legally sufficient.

Recommended fix: none

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the *Dingwall* reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money from the shelter, that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable to protect his mother. Does the *Dingwall* approach allow Holmes to satisfy the imminence requirement for duress? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because *Dingwall* established that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the *Dingwall* reasoning relaxing the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former associates.
(c) Yes, because *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the *Dingwall* exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is committed.
(e) No, because the *Dingwall* exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or third party.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens *Dingwall* into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that duress under *Dingwall* cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that the core logic of *Dingwall*—that prolonged, unpredictable threats create a psychological state where danger feels constantly imminent—is not inherently limited to domestic relationships. If the psychological mechanism holds true, any sustained pattern of severe threats (like Nietzsche's months of extortion) should theoretically satisfy the imminence requirement under a broad, functional reading of the case.
(b) Argument-for: The actual holding in *Dingwall* is carefully circumscribed to the context of battering and coercive control (usually tied to intimate partner violence). Expanding this subjective standard to run-of-the-mill criminal disputes or gang extortion would functionally destroy the temporal imminence requirement. Therefore, Holmes cannot invoke *Dingwall* for generalized associate disputes.
(c) Argument-for: A student might reason that by allowing a duress instruction even when the coercer is absent, *Dingwall* functionally eliminates the temporal imminence requirement in favor of evaluating the credibility of future threats based on past violence. Since Nietzsche has a track record of threats, Holmes could rely on this functional elimination of strict imminence.
(d) Argument-for: A student could believe that courts rigidly cabin exceptions like *Dingwall* to prevent abuse of the defense. They might argue that to satisfy imminence without the coercer's immediate presence, the defendant must be physically confined or isolated by the coercer at the time of the crime, proving there was absolutely no opportunity to escape.
(e) Argument-for: Because *Dingwall* is rooted in the subjective psychological trauma of the battered defendant, a student could plausibly argue the exception only applies when the defendant's own body is threatened. Threats to third parties might not trigger the specific psychological paralysis and learned helplessness that justifies relaxing the traditional rule.

The distractors are effectively locked with explicit, falsifiable legal claims. Option (a) incorrectly claims the rule applies "regardless of the relationship." Option (c) falsely states the case "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely." Option (d) directly contradicts *Dingwall* by requiring the defendant to be "physically confined... at the exact moment" of the offense. Option (e) falsely excludes threats against family members, contradicting foundational duress principles which *Dingwall* did not overturn.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "regardless of the relationship between the coercer and the defendant" — wrong because *Dingwall* expressly grounded its reasoning in the specific context of battering and coercive control relationships.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" — wrong because *Dingwall* merely allows expert testimony to inform whether a defendant reasonably perceived the threat as imminent, rather than eliminating the imminence element itself.
- (d): "strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined... at the exact moment" — wrong because the primary holding of *Dingwall* is that the coercer need not be physically present or actively restraining the defendant at the time of the crime.
- (e): "strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because duress doctrine universally recognizes threats to immediate family members, and *Dingwall* does not restrict this baseline rule.

Recommended fix: none

## Q06

### Grounding

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald. Based on the investigation, Markov struck Wald while acting under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was a government assassin trying to poison the residents. Afterward, Markov told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov understood he was physically striking a person, but because of an acute psychotic episode, he could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person. This cognitive failure alone satisfies the MPC's threshold requirement for an acquittal.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the physical nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong, precluding any claim of lacking substantial capacity.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is therefore completely excluded from the definition of a qualifying mental disease or defect under the explicit limiting provisions of MPC Section 4.01(2).
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives.
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong, which automatically satisfies the MPC rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality/wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and, because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives, he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his act. (a) is incorrect because the "nature and quality" test refers to understanding the physical act itself, which Markov understood (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical nature of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness; his statement evidences his delusion. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC § 4.01(2) exclusion targets psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not psychotic disorders. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses the "lacks substantial capacity to conform his conduct" standard, not the "entirely destroyed" standard of the irresistible impulse test, and the facts establish a cognitive rather than volitional impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC appreciate criminality, Ch 23, Refinement `mpc-appreciate-criminality`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

(a) Argument-for: Under the MPC, a cognitive failure due to a mental disease can support an insanity defense. ### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald. Based on the investigation, Markov struck Wald while acting under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was a government assassin trying to poison the residents. Afterward, Markov told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov understood he was physically striking a person, but because of an acute psychotic episode, he could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person. This cognitive failure alone satisfies the MPC's threshold requirement for an acquittal.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the physical nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong, precluding any claim of lacking substantial capacity.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is therefore completely excluded from the definition of a qualifying mental disease or defect under the explicit limiting provisions of MPC Section 4.01(2).
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives.
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong, which automatically satisfies the MPC rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality/wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and, because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives, he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his act. (a) is incorrect because the "nature and quality" test refers to understanding the physical act itself, which Markov understood (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical nature of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness; his statement evidences his delusion. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC § 4.01(2) exclusion targets psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not psychotic disorders. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses the "lacks substantial capacity to conform his conduct" standard, not the "entirely destroyed" standard of the irresistible impulse test, and the facts establish a cognitive rather than volitional impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) could be argued as correct because a student might believe that understanding the "nature and quality" of an act involves understanding its true context, not just the physical motions. Since Markov thought he was stopping an assassin, he fundamentally misunderstood the identity of his victim and the reality of the situation. Under this broad interpretation, a student could argue he lacked the requisite cognitive understanding of what he was doing. This would supposedly satisfy the MPC's threshold requirement for an acquittal.
(b) Argument-for: Option (b) could be argued as correct if one assumes that understanding the mechanics of an assault automatically precludes an insanity defense. Because Markov stated "I was stopping an assassination," he clearly formed a goal-oriented intent to commit violence. A student might conclude that this level of awareness demonstrates he knew the act itself was legally wrong, even if his motive was delusional. Thus, this awareness would supposedly negate the claim that he lacked substantial capacity.
(c) Argument-for: Option (c) relies on a misapplication of the MPC's exclusion for psychopathy. A student could argue that because Markov's action was violent and antisocial, his underlying condition might fall under the exclusion in MPC § 4.01(2) for abnormalities manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct. If schizophrenia is mischaracterized as simply a pattern of dangerous behavior, a student could incorrectly conclude the defense is entirely barred by the statute.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) correctly applies the MPC insanity framework to the facts. The MPC excuses a defendant who, due to a mental disease or defect, lacks substantial capacity to appreciate the criminality/wrongfulness of their conduct. The prompt establishes that Markov suffers from schizophrenia and, due to a delusion that he was saving lives from an assassin, could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. This aligns perfectly with the cognitive prong of the MPC test.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) could be chosen by a student focusing on the volitional aspect of the insanity defense. Because the delusion was so overwhelming, one might argue Markov could not have stopped himself, seemingly satisfying the older "irresistible impulse" test. Since the MPC's volitional standard ("lacks substantial capacity to conform") is broader and easier to meet than the strict irresistible impulse test, a student might reason that satisfying the stricter test automatically satisfies the MPC's volitional prong, making (e) a correct conclusion.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is the only legally correct answer, directly mapping the stipulated facts (schizophrenia, inability to appreciate wrongfulness) to the MPC standard. Distractor (a) is falsifiable because it incorrectly claims Markov did not know the "nature and quality" of his act, which contradicts the prompt's explicit statement that he "understood he was physically striking a person." Distractor (b) is falsifiable because it draws a legally false equivalence, claiming that understanding the physical nature of an act means the defendant "therefore knew what he was doing was wrong." Distractor (c) contains an explicit false legal claim that schizophrenia is excluded by MPC § 4.01(2), which actually targets psychopathy. Distractor (e) incorrectly states that the facts satisfy the irresistible impulse test and wrongly attributes a volitional defense to facts that expressly establish a cognitive defect ("could not appreciate that his actions were wrong").

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "he did not know the nature and quality of his act" — wrong because the prompt explicitly states he "understood he was physically striking a person," meaning he knew the physical nature/quality of the act, and the terminology is drawn from M'Naghten, not the MPC.
- (b): "understood the physical nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong" — wrong because understanding the physical mechanics of an act does not legally equate to appreciating its wrongfulness under the MPC, especially when the defendant is operating under a delusion.
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia is a condition 'manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct'" — wrong because this is an explicit, false legal claim; the MPC § 4.01(2) exclusion limits psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not schizophrenic psychotic disorders.
- (e): "satisfying the irresistible impulse test... which automatically satisfies the MPC rule" — wrong because the prompt's facts stipulate a cognitive failure ("could not appreciate... wrong"), not a volitional failure to control behavior, and the MPC utilizes a different volitional standard ("conform his conduct").

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q07

### Grounding

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and his acute psychotic episode that severely impaired his reality testing, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either jurisdiction's specific statutory formulation.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was firing a weapon—his delusions about justification would be entirely irrelevant under IDRA's strictly limited, single-prong cognitive approach to the insanity defense.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's paranoid schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be legally mandated to acquit him based on the prosecution's failure to meet its heavy burden.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold.

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation. Because Markov was not physically incapacitated, his delusions and command hallucinations would be deemed insufficient to establish an insanity defense under the federal system's strictly limited diagnostic framework.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Federal IDRA requirements, Ch 23, Refinement `federal-idra-requirements`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. <!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that both the MPC and IDRA focus heavily on the cognitive aspect of insanity. Because both standards inquire into whether the defendant could appreciate the wrongfulness of the conduct, one could incorrectly assume that the IDRA merely codified the MPC's cognitive prong without changing its wording. Under this assumption, the tests would be functionally identical, and Markov's severe psychosis would easily satisfy the "substantial capacity" language in both.
(b) Argument-for: A student might recall that the Hinckley verdict sparked massive backlash aimed at restricting the insanity defense, leading Congress to look back toward the older, stricter M'Naghten rule. Given M'Naghten's famous emphasis on whether the defendant knew the "nature and quality" of the act, a student might erroneously conclude that IDRA eliminated the "wrongfulness" prong entirely to prevent defendants like Markov—who physically understood they were firing a weapon but had delusions about justification—from escaping liability.
(c) Argument-for: A student could correctly recall that historically, under the pre-Hinckley federal standard established by cases like Davis v. United States, the prosecution did indeed carry the burden to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt once the defense was raised. If the student fails to realize that the IDRA explicitly reversed this burden allocation, they would logically conclude that the IDRA makes the defense incredibly easy to establish compared to modern state MPC formulations.
(d) Argument-for: This is the correct answer because it accurately catalogues the four distinct changes the IDRA made to narrow the insanity defense: adding the "severe" modifier to mental disease, removing the volitional prong, heightening "substantial capacity" to "unable to appreciate," and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. It also correctly applies the law to the facts, noting that despite these higher hurdles, a defendant suffering from an acute psychotic episode of paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations would likely still qualify under the strict IDRA standard.
(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that the IDRA's addition of the word "severe" was intended to be highly exclusionary, weeding out almost all mental health claims. Taking this to an extreme, they could interpret "severe" as requiring an almost total detachment from physical reality or outright physical incapacitation, assuming that disorders where the defendant can still execute complex physical tasks (like firing a gun) are categorically barred. Under this hyper-strict interpretation, Markov's physical capability would defeat his defense.

Head-to-head: 
Option (d) correctly identifies all statutory elements of the IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) and accurately concludes that Markov's severe diagnosis still likely satisfies them. Every distractor features an explicitly falsifiable misstatement of black-letter federal law: (a) wrongly claims IDRA uses the MPC's "substantial capacity" test; (b) wrongly claims IDRA eliminated the wrongfulness prong; (c) relies on the obsolete pre-Hinckley rule to wrongly claim the prosecution bears the burden of proof beyond a reasonable doubt; and (e) fabricates a legal requirement that "severe" mental disease demands total physical incapacitation while categorically excluding paranoid schizophrenia. The distractors are securely false, making (d) the unequivocally correct answer.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity...'" — wrong because the IDRA standard explicitly abandoned the MPC's "substantial capacity" standard in favor of an absolute "unable to appreciate" standard.
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17 explicitly states the defendant must be unable to appreciate the nature and quality "or the wrongfulness" of his acts.
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because the IDRA shifted the burden of proof to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" — wrong because paranoid schizophrenia is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease under IDRA, and the statute contains no "total incapacitation" requirement.

Recommended fix: none

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and his acute psychotic episode that severely impaired his reality testing, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either jurisdiction's specific statutory formulation.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was firing a weapon—his delusions about justification would be entirely irrelevant under IDRA's strictly limited, single-prong cognitive approach to the insanity defense.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's paranoid schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be legally mandated to acquit him based on the prosecution's failure to meet its heavy burden.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold.

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation. Because Markov was not physically incapacitated, his delusions and command hallucinations would be deemed insufficient to establish an insanity defense under the federal system's strictly limited diagnostic framework.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option posits that IDRA and the MPC are fundamentally the same because they both revolve around evaluating the defendant's cognitive capacity. One could argue that, in practice, courts assessing whether someone is "unable to appreciate" wrongfulness versus whether they "lack substantial capacity to appreciate" wrongfulness are doing exactly the same fact-finding regarding cognitive state. Therefore, the cognitive tests are functionally "identical" across the two standards.
(b) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies that IDRA heavily restricted the insanity defense post-Hinckley. A student might argue that by reverting to a strict M'Naghten-like standard, Congress intended to narrow the inquiry exclusively to whether the defendant knew the physical nature and quality of the act (e.g., that a gun fires bullets). Thus, eliminating the broader "wrongfulness" prong would serve IDRA's legislative intent to limit the defense.
(c) Argument-for: This option correctly notes that burdens of proof are a major part of insanity doctrine. Before IDRA, under the federal rule established in *Davis v. United States*, once a defendant raised the insanity defense, the burden shifted to the prosecution to prove sanity beyond a reasonable doubt. A student could argue that IDRA merely codified this long-standing federal standard, making the defense easier in federal court than under the MPC.
(d) Argument-for: This option correctly recites all the key mechanical changes instituted by 18 U.S.C. § 17 (IDRA). It correctly identifies the addition of the "severe" modifier to mental disease, the elimination of the volitional prong, the change from "substantial capacity" to "unable," and the shift of the burden of proof to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. It also accurately concludes that severe paranoid schizophrenia typically still qualifies even under this strict formulation.
(e) Argument-for: This option focuses on IDRA's unique statutory requirement of a "severe" mental disease. A student could argue that to give independent meaning to the word "severe," Congress must have intended to exclude standard DSM-listed psychoses unless accompanied by complete incapacitation, effectively barring most schizophrenia claims.

Head-to-head:
Option (d) is legally perfectly accurate, identifying the precise statutory changes made by the Insanity Defense Reform Act of 1984. Option (a) fails because IDRA explicitly uses the word "unable," not "substantial capacity." Option (b) fails because IDRA explicitly retains the wrongfulness prong ("nature and quality or the wrongfulness"). Option (c) fails because it gets the burden of proof exactly backward; IDRA shifted the burden from the prosecution to the defendant. All distractors feature robust, objectively falsifiable claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity to appreciate'" — wrong because IDRA explicitly changed the standard to "unable to appreciate."
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17(a) requires proof that the defendant was unable to appreciate "the nature and quality or the wrongfulness of his acts."
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because IDRA affirmatively shifted the burden to the defendant (clear and convincing evidence).
- (e): "excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" — wrong because paranoid schizophrenia is categorically recognized as a "severe" mental disease under IDRA.

## Q08

### Grounding

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Mens Rea Model Abolition / Kahler v. Kansas, Ch 23, Refinements `mens-rea-model-abolition` and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
Recommended fix: n/a

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that if a state abolishes the insanity defense, it logically removes clinical psychiatric evidence from the guilt phase entirely to prevent backdoor insanity claims. Under this strict reading, the jurisdiction would treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant, making Markov's records strictly inadmissible for any purpose, regardless of his capacity to form intent.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue this based on a constitutional fundamental-fairness theory. They might mistakenly recall *Kahler v. Kansas* as establishing a constitutional floor rather than a ceiling, arguing that deeply rooted historical traditions under the Due Process Clause require every state to maintain at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to protect mentally ill defendants.
(c) Argument-for: This correctly applies the *mens rea* model upheld in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Under this model, mental illness is only relevant to the extent it negates the statutory mens rea. Because Markov's delusion involved stopping an assassin, he clearly formed the purpose and knowledge to cause bodily injury; his inability to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions is irrelevant to the mens rea inquiry.
(d) Argument-for: An advocate could argue that the mens rea model inherently incorporates subjective justifications into the intent inquiry. Under this theory, if a severe mental illness causes a sincere delusional belief that violence is legally justified (like defense of others against an assassin), it vitiates the culpable mens rea, thus providing a complete affirmative defense.
(e) Argument-for: A student might contend that while the formal affirmative defense of insanity is abolished, evidentiary rules still allow defendants to introduce all relevant psychiatric evidence to challenge their general criminal responsibility. Under this view, abolition states would permit psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on moral culpability at the trial stage to allow the jury to assess the totality of the defendant's mental state.

Head-to-head: Option (c) is the only legally accurate application of the mens rea model upheld in *Kahler v. Kansas*, correctly identifying that a delusional motive does not negate the intent to cause physical harm. Option (a) makes the demonstrably false claim that mental illness evidence is strictly inadmissible for any purpose, ignoring the rule that it can negate mens rea. Option (b) states the exact opposite of the Supreme Court's holding in *Kahler*. Option (d) incorrectly invents an affirmative defense of delusional justification under the mens rea model. Option (e) falsely claims abolition states admit wrongfulness evidence for all purposes at the trial stage, contradicting the core limitation of the mens rea model.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent" — wrong because the mens rea model explicitly allows psychiatric evidence to negate the statutory mens rea.
- (b): "the Supreme Court held in Kahler v. Kansas that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" — wrong because *Kahler* explicitly held that the Due Process Clause does *not* require states to adopt a wrongfulness test.
- (d): "the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief" — wrong because the mens rea model does not create a freestanding affirmative defense for delusional justification; it only allows evidence to negate statutory intent.
- (e): "abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage" — wrong because abolition states strictly limit this evidence to negating mens rea at the trial (guilt) stage, excluding evidence bearing solely on moral culpability.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might reason that if a state has "abolished the insanity defense," it reflects a legislative judgment that mental illness has no place in assessing guilt. Thus, courts in those jurisdictions would logically exclude all psychiatric evidence to prevent the defense from sneaking insanity concepts through the back door.
(b) Argument-for: A student might incorrectly recall the outcome of *Kahler v. Kansas*, confusing the petitioner's argument with the Court's holding. Since the Eighth Amendment prohibits cruel and unusual punishment, one could persuasively but incorrectly argue the Supreme Court established a constitutional baseline requiring states to assess a defendant's capacity to recognize wrongfulness.
(c) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the mens rea model validated in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Since Markov intended the physical act of causing bodily injury to another person, he fulfilled the statutory mens rea of "purposely or knowingly." Because his delusion primarily impacts his understanding of whether the act was morally or legally wrong—an inquiry precluded by the abolition of the insanity defense—his evidence is inadmissible to defeat the charge.
(d) Argument-for: A student might conflate the mens rea model with a subjective justification defense. If Markov sincerely believed he was stopping an assassin, a student could argue he lacked the true "criminal intent" necessary for assault, reasoning that the mens rea model inherently accommodates delusional self-defense or defense of others.
(e) Argument-for: A student might assume that "abolishing the insanity defense" simply means downgrading it from an affirmative defense to an evidentiary rule where all psychiatric evidence is weighed by the jury to assess general moral culpability. Under this view, abolition merely changes the procedural mechanism, not the substantive relevance of wrongfulness.

Head-to-head: 
Option (c) accurately synthesizes the rule from *Kahler v. Kansas*: in a mens rea model jurisdiction, psychiatric evidence is only admissible if it defeats the specific mental state required by the statute, which a delusion of justification does not do. The distractors are decisively flawed. Option (a) falsely claims an absolute evidentiary bar. Option (b) legally reverses the actual holding of *Kahler*. Option (d) fabricates a complete affirmative defense under the mens rea model. Option (e) contradicts the core definition of an abolition jurisdiction by claiming moral wrongfulness evidence remains admissible. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose" — wrong because mens rea model states explicitly allow this evidence to negate the statutory mens rea.
- (b): "the Supreme Court held in Kahler v. Kansas that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" — wrong because *Kahler* held the exact opposite, ruling that Due Process does not mandate a wrongfulness test.
- (d): "the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief" — wrong because the mens rea model provides no such affirmative defense and solely limits the inquiry to the elements of the offense.
- (e): "abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability" — wrong because abolition states expressly prohibit evidence admitted solely to show an inability to appreciate moral wrongfulness.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q09

### Grounding

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior.

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — personality-disorder-exclusion and co-occurring-causation, Ch 23, Refinement `personality-disorder-exclusion` and `co-occurring-causation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior.

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior.

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.
