### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior.

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that the MPC’s explicit carve-out for psychopathy or antisocial personality disorder serves as a bright-line policy rule designed to hold individuals with antisocial traits accountable. From this perspective, allowing a defendant with antisocial personality disorder to raise the insanity defense would circumvent legislative intent, effectively acting as an absolute taint or bar whenever the excluded condition is present and contributing to the crime.
(b) Argument-for: A student might reason that modern forensic psychiatry often views comorbid diagnoses as part of an integrated symptom profile rather than wholly severable entities. Consequently, they might assume the MPC accommodates this clinical reality through a merger doctrine, preventing courts from engaging in the artificial task of parsing out the exact causal fraction attributable to each distinct label. 
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that because severe psychotic disorders like schizophrenia inherently involve a break from reality, their mere presence is so overwhelming that it legally forecloses any debate over causation. Under this view, once the threshold of a severe qualifying defect is met alongside a non-qualifying disorder, the law presumes the psychotic illness drove the incapacity, making further causal inquiries irrelevant.
(d) Argument-for: A student would recognize this as the exact doctrinal mechanism of MPC § 4.01(2). The code explicitly excludes abnormalities manifested "only" by repeated antisocial conduct. Thus, while antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, its presence does not nullify a co-occurring qualifying mental disease like schizophrenia, provided the schizophrenia actually caused the requisite substantial impairment.
(e) Argument-for: A student might analogize to strict concurrent causation principles in torts or other areas of criminal law, arguing that if the non-qualifying, excluded disorder (ASPD) was a necessary but-for cause of the violent behavior, the defendant's conduct was fatally tainted by an unexcused trait. Thus, any reliance on the non-qualifying condition severs the exclusive causal link needed for an insanity acquittal.

Head-to-head: Option (d) correctly articulates the threshold-versus-causation distinction central to the MPC's insanity framework, correctly noting that the "manifested only by" language (often called the psychopathy exclusion) does not disqualify defendants with a genuine co-occurring mental disease. The four distractors are uniformly excellent because each presents a highly plausible but legally fictitious standard to deal with comorbidity. Furthermore, every distractor is firmly locked with strict, falsifiable language ("absolute bar," "automatically merge," "automatically satisfies," "defeat the defense entirely"), making them objectively incorrect under established doctrine.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim" — wrong because MPC § 4.01(2) excludes abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct; it does not bar claims based on co-occurring qualifying illnesses.
- (b): "automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because no such statutory merger doctrine exists in the MPC; the trier of fact must determine whether the qualifying disease caused the incapacity.
- (c): "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defendant still bears the burden of proving that the qualifying mental disease actually caused the substantial incapacity.
- (e): "prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because concurrent causation involving a qualifying mental disease can still support a valid insanity defense; the qualifying disease does not have to be the sole cause.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior.

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might defend this option by arguing that the MPC's explicit exclusion of conditions manifested by antisocial conduct reflects a broad policy judgment against granting insanity to psychopathic defendants. Under this view, allowing a defendant with a co-occurring personality disorder to claim insanity would create a massive loophole, subverting legislative intent. Therefore, a student could plausibly believe that the presence of antisocial personality disorder acts as an absolute taint, categorically barring the defense regardless of other diagnoses.
(b) Argument-for: A student could assert that because psychiatric conditions often overlap and are difficult to cleanly disentangle, the law treats a defendant's mental state holistically. Under this theory, the MPC merges co-occurring diagnoses to avoid arbitrary battles over which specific disorder caused which specific thought. Thus, if a qualifying disorder is present alongside a personality disorder, the two conditions automatically merge into a single legally cognizable "mental disease or defect."
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that the presence of schizophrenia—a severe, universally recognized psychotic disorder—is so profound that it inherently satisfies the insanity defense's requirements. From this perspective, the law presumes that such a severe loss of contact with reality necessarily drives the defendant's behavior, rendering any inquiry into the relative contribution of a personality disorder moot. Therefore, a co-occurring psychotic disorder would automatically satisfy the causation element for all purposes.
(d) Argument-for: This is the correct legal standard under the MPC framework. A student would recognize that MPC § 4.01(2) excludes abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct, meaning antisocial personality disorder alone cannot qualify. However, because the word "only" limits the exclusion, a co-occurring qualifying illness like schizophrenia clears the threshold requirement. The defense therefore survives as long as the schizophrenia caused the requisite incapacity, even if the personality disorder was a contributing factor.
(e) Argument-for: A student could logically rely on standard principles of causation to argue that an excluded condition cannot be a necessary link in the causal chain. If the prosecution proves the antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause, it means the qualifying mental illness alone was insufficient to cause the conduct. A student might conclude that under the MPC, any causal contribution from an expressly excluded condition severs the legal link between the disease and the incapacity, wholly defeating the defense.

Head-to-head: Option (d) correctly states the operation of MPC § 4.01(2) by acknowledging that the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement (leaving room for co-occurring qualifying diseases) while requiring that the qualifying disease actually cause the incapacity. The distractors are decisively false because they rely on fabricated absolute doctrines: (a) creates an absolute bar for any personality disorder diagnosis, contradicting the word "only" in the MPC; (b) invents an "automatic merger" doctrine for psychiatric conditions; (c) wrongly claims a psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element" to moot the inquiry; and (e) falsely imports a strict "but-for" causation rule that defeats the defense entirely if the ASPD contributed at all.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness" — wrong because MPC § 4.01(2) only excludes an abnormality manifested *only* by antisocial conduct; a co-occurring qualifying illness bypasses this exclusion.
- (b): "the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because the MPC contains no automatic merger doctrine; disorders remain legally distinct for the threshold inquiry.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defense must still affirmatively prove that the qualifying disease caused the substantial lack of capacity; the inquiry is not rendered irrelevant.
- (e): "the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role" — wrong because the defense survives if the qualifying disease caused the requisite incapacity, even if the personality disorder was also a contributing cause.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q10

### Grounding

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. This categorical prohibition operates as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase of the trial.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress murder bar, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. This categorical prohibition operates as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase of the trial.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the strict common law rule forbidding duress as a murder defense has been conceptually reimagined in some contexts as an imperfect defense or a sentencing mitigator. To avoid a profoundly unfair result at the guilt phase, courts sometimes treat mitigating factors as reducing culpability rather than barring liability completely. By stating the "murder bar" operates only to limit exposure after conviction, this option offers a plausible compromise. It reconciles the rigid common law tradition with the reality of a highly credible threat to a close relative.
(b) Argument-for: This accurately reflects the core traditional common law doctrine. Under the common law, duress is simply not a defense to intentional murder, nor is it a defense to felony murder. The fundamental rationale is that the law cannot endorse balancing an innocent life against another. Therefore, the defense is categorically unavailable as a substantive bar at the guilt phase. This applies regardless of how severe, imminent, or credible the threat is.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that the Model Penal Code represents the definitive modern understanding of duress. Under this view, the MPC's progressive approach has effectively eclipsed the traditional common law in contemporary practice. By pointing to a "modern trend" that allows duress for all crimes, this option appeals to students who believe the common law's rigid murder bar has been universally abandoned. If it were true that all jurisdictions allow this, the jury could fully acquit Holmes.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that strict historical common law rules required the threat of harm to be strictly imminent and direct. They could reasonably deduce that the threat must be to the defendant himself rather than a third party to satisfy this immediacy. By arguing that the common law "requires that the threat target the defendant personally," this option provides a technically strict rule-based reason to deny the defense. This sounds like an authentic common law technicality that categorically defeats the defense.
(e) Argument-for: A student could reason that felony murder strictly requires an underlying predicate felony to anchor liability. If that underlying felony is completely excused by duress, the felony murder charge must logically fail because its foundation is gone. By stating that duress "always breaks the chain of felony murder liability," this option provides a compelling logical syllogism. The claim that this happens in "every jurisdiction" sounds like a definitive hornbook rule that cleanly resolves the tension between the murder bar and a coerced predicate act.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear, unambiguous correct answer, correctly applying the traditional common law rule that completely bars duress as a defense to any murder. The distractors are remarkably well-constructed because they all anchor into plausible misunderstandings of doctrine, yet they all feature explicitly false, locked legal statements. Option (a) incorrectly claims the murder bar is just a sentencing-phase consideration. Option (c) applies the MPC despite the prompt specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend in "all jurisdictions." Option (d) falsely claims the common law strictly requires the threat to target the defendant personally. Option (e) uses absolute phrasing to falsely claim duress "always" breaks the felony murder chain in "every jurisdiction," which fundamentally conflicts with the traditional common law rule.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction... not a substantive bar at the guilt phase" — wrong because the common law murder bar is an absolute substantive defense bar at the guilt phase.
- (c): "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because many jurisdictions, specifically those following the common law, continue to maintain a strict bar against duress for murder.
- (d): "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law broadly allows duress to be claimed for threats of death or serious bodily injury to immediate family members or close relations.
- (e): "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction" — wrong because the traditional common law rule (and the majority rule) is that duress is categorically unavailable for murder, including felony murder, meaning it does not break the chain in every jurisdiction.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might select this option if they believe that the harshness of the traditional common law murder bar has been universally softened by modern jurisprudence into a mere sentencing mitigator. In this view, the guilt phase would still permit the defense so long as the elements of duress are met, with the homicide aspect only limiting exposure post-conviction. This relies on misinterpreting modern sentencing reforms as having retroactively altered the substantive common law guilt-phase bar.
(b) Argument-for: This option accurately reflects the traditional common law rule. Under the common law, duress is never a defense to murder. This prohibition is absolute and categorical, serving as a substantive bar at the guilt phase regardless of how severe the threat was. It applies equally to felony murder, preventing the defendant from escaping liability for the taking of an innocent life.
(c) Argument-for: A student might choose this option if they incorrectly prioritize the Model Penal Code's approach over the stated common law facts. By blending the MPC rule with an exaggerated "modern trend," the student could argue that the jury should be allowed to weigh the threat's credibility against the murder charge to fully acquit.
(d) Argument-for: A student might select this option by strictly conflating early common law rules with self-defense requirements. Option (e) uses absolute language ("in every jurisdiction") to describe a minority rule regarding felony murder and duress. The keyed answer (b) is factually and legally the only correct option, and all distractors contain strictly falsifiable claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration" — wrong because the common law murder bar acts as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase.
- (c): "modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because many jurisdictions firmly retain the traditional murder bar, making the "all jurisdictions" claim factually false.

- (e): "coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense" — wrong because many jurisdictions hold that duress does not break the chain of felony murder, making the "every jurisdiction" modifier objectively false.

## Q11

### Grounding

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Necessity fault calibration (MPC vs. Common Law), Ch 21, Refinement `mpc-fault-calibration`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue this option is correct by interpreting *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* as a broad prohibition on medical necessity defenses for all heavily regulated drugs. The reasoning would be that the legislative act of placing a drug on a controlled substances schedule reflects a comprehensive balancing of medical utility versus harm. Therefore, any judicial weighing of harms regarding that drug is strictly preempted, rendering the necessity defense categorically unavailable for unauthorized possession.
(b) Argument-for: This option appeals to the core animating principles of the necessity defense: proportionality and lack of alternatives. A student could argue that preventing a severe mental health crisis (a psychotic break) objectively outweighs the minor property damage of breaking a pharmacy window. Given the immediate unavailability of an ER bed, Kant had no lawful recourse, thereby satisfying both elements and making the defense viable regardless of standard, assuming the expiration of the prescription is viewed as too remote to constitute legally relevant "fault."
(c) Argument-for: This keyed answer accurately reflects the stark jurisdictional split regarding a defendant's fault in creating an emergency. Under the traditional common law's "clean hands" doctrine, contributing to the crisis categorically defeats the necessity defense. Conversely, Model Penal Code § 3.02(2) specifically calibrates the fault bar to the mens rea of the charged offense; because Kant is charged with intentional and knowing crimes, his negligent or reckless failure to renew his prescription does not strip him of the justification. 
(d) Argument-for: A student could read this option as stating a rigid but plausible statutory limitation, assuming the MPC adopted a punitive stance toward defendants who create their own emergencies. The argument would assert that § 3.02 treats recklessness or negligence in bringing about the crisis as an absolute forfeiture of the justification defense. Under this view, once the defendant improperly creates the need to offend, the specific mens rea of the subsequently charged crime becomes irrelevant, and the defense fails categorically.
(e) Argument-for: A student could deduce this rule by conflating the requirements of the necessity defense with those of duress or self-defense. The argument posits that traditional common law is highly restrictive and reserves the necessity defense for only the most dire physical emergencies, strictly requiring a threat of death or serious bodily injury (as in *The Queen v. Dudley and Stephens*). Under this logic, averting a psychotic break—a severe medical condition but not an immediate physical death threat—fails the common-law test but passes the MPC's broader "choice of evils" standard.

Head-to-head: Option (c) is the legally accurate keyed answer, cleanly distinguishing the common law "clean hands" rule from the MPC's mens rea fault calibration. Distractor (a) overextends *Oakland Cannabis*, which involved the specific legislative findings of Schedule I (no accepted medical use), not all scheduled prescription drugs generally. Distractor (b) fails because it ignores the common law's strict prohibition against contributing to the emergency. Distractor (d) directly contradicts the plain text of MPC § 3.02(2), which explicitly preserves the defense for intentional crimes if the defendant was merely reckless or negligent in creating the crisis. Distractor (e) introduces a fabricated limitation, as common-law necessity merely requires the averted harm to outweigh the committed harm, reserving the strict "death or serious bodily injury" requirement for duress, not necessity.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim" — wrong because *Oakland Cannabis* established legislative preemption based on the specific statutory finding that Schedule I drugs have "no currently accepted medical use," not merely the scheduling of any prescription drug.
- (b): "The defense is viable under both standards" — wrong because under the traditional common law, Kant's fault in creating the emergency categorically bars the defense under the clean-hands rule, rendering it not viable under both.
- (d): "categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent... regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) explicitly calibrates the forfeiture of the defense to the mens rea of the charged offense, preserving it for intentional crimes.
- (e): "traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury" — wrong because common-law necessity requires only that the harm averted be greater than the harm caused; the strict requirement of death or serious bodily injury is a characteristic of common-law duress.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative*, the Supreme Court held that a medical necessity defense cannot succeed when the legislature has already weighed the harms by classifying a substance under the Controlled Substances Act. Since Kant is charged with unauthorized possession of a controlled substance, a student could argue that the legislative scheduling categorically preempts the judicial weighing of harms required for a necessity defense. In this view, any claim of medical necessity is foreclosed by the statutory scheme.
(b) Argument-for: The core of the necessity defense is the choice of evils. A student could argue that averting a severe medical crisis like a psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage of breaking into a pharmacy and the victimless crime of possessing a drug. Since Kant had no legal alternative (assuming no ER beds were available), the defense's primary elements are satisfied. Thus, the defense should be viable under both the common law and MPC, treating his failure to renew his prescription as mere background context rather than an absolute bar.
(c) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the MPC's nuanced approach to fault in creating the emergency. Under traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the crisis entirely bars the necessity defense. However, MPC § 3.02(2) calibrates fault: if the defendant is negligent or reckless in bringing about the situation, the defense is only barred for crimes where negligence or recklessness suffices. Because Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing possession, his lesser fault in creating the emergency does not bar his defense.
(d) Argument-for: A student could interpret the MPC's fault provision as a strict, categorical rule against rewarding defendants who create their own emergencies. If a defendant's negligence or recklessness brings about the crisis, they shouldn't be allowed to invoke necessity to escape punishment for subsequent intentional acts. Therefore, one could argue that § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is at fault, regardless of the mens rea of the ultimate charge.
(e) Argument-for: Common law justification defenses are often narrower than the MPC. A student could conflate the elements of necessity with duress, or assume that traditional courts strictly limited necessity to physical emergencies. Under this reasoning, common law necessity strictly requires an imminent threat of death or serious bodily injury, meaning a psychiatric emergency like a psychotic break would only qualify under the broader "choice of evils" standard of the MPC.

Head-to-head: Option (c) accurately captures the legal distinction between the common law's absolute fault bar and the MPC's mens rea-calibrated fault rule. Option (a) overextends *Oakland Cannabis*, which only applies to Schedule I drugs (where Congress explicitly found "no currently accepted medical use"), not standard prescription drugs. Option (b) falsely claims viability under both standards, ignoring the common-law absolute bar for creating the emergency. Option (d) misstates the MPC rule by making it a categorical bar, directly contradicting the statutory text of § 3.02(2). Option (e) falsely imports the "death or serious bodily injury" requirement from duress into common-law necessity, which generally allows any lesser harm to be chosen over a greater harm. All distractors contain clear, falsifiable legal errors locked with absolute phrasing.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim" — wrong because *Oakland Cannabis* applies only to Schedule I drugs (which have "no currently accepted medical use" by definition), not to the mere scheduling of any prescription drug.
- (b): "viable under both standards" — wrong because the traditional common law categorically bars the necessity defense if the defendant contributed to creating the emergency.
- (d): "categorically strips the justification defense... regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) explicitly preserves the necessity defense for intentional/knowing crimes if the defendant was merely reckless/negligent in creating the crisis.
- (e): "traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury" — wrong because common-law necessity compares the relative magnitude of harms and is not strictly limited to threats of death or serious bodily injury.

## Q12

### Grounding

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-threat-threshold` and `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

<!-- edge-case-audit: CLEAN

3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass (The 5-day delay parallels the imminence issue in Q4, but by scoping the inquiry strictly to threshold and target scope, the question does not conflict with Q4's ruling on imminence.)
