### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) accurately reflects the doctrinal baseline for duress under both the common law and the Model Penal Code. The common law requires a threat of present, imminent death or serious bodily injury. A threat to put someone in the hospital for surgical repair easily constitutes "serious bodily injury," and a mother classically qualifies as a "close relation," satisfying the common law's target scope. The MPC is even broader, requiring only a threat of "unlawful force" against "any person," which clearly covers both the beating and the mother, making the elements satisfied under both regimes.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that option (b) is correct based on a rigid, historical interpretation of the common law. Early strict common-law formulations required the threat of force to be directed at the defendant personally to excuse criminal liability, theorizing that threats against third parties afforded the defendant an opportunity to seek legal recourse. If a jurisdiction rigidly adheres to this traditional formulation that duress excuses only when the actor's own life or limb is threatened, then threats to the mother would not suffice. Therefore, stating that coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is legally insufficient would accurately describe this narrowest historical view.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that option (c) correctly identifies an implicit imminence failure under the MPC. The MPC's duress defense under § 2.09 requires that a person of reasonable firmness in the actor's situation would have been unable to resist. A threat made on March 10 for an act to be committed on March 15 affords a five-day window to seek police protection or escape. One could argue that because the danger was not present and unavoidable at the time of the crime, the MPC functionally retains an imminence requirement to ensure the threat genuinely overcame the actor's will.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) can be defended by pointing to the exceptionally strict nature of the common-law threshold, which historically requires a threat of death or serious bodily harm that is immediately impending. Some restrictive interpretations essentially demand a "gun-to-the-head" scenario to satisfy both the imminence and severity prongs simultaneously. Since Bentham's threat was primarily a future promise of violence via a third-party enforcer, a student could argue it does not rise to the level of an immediate, unavoidable threat of deadly force strictly required to overcome criminal culpability at common law.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue for (e) by focusing on the legal intersection of "reasonable firmness" and the opportunity to escape. Under both the common law and the MPC, if a defendant has a clear, safe avenue of escape—such as five days to report a threat to the police—the duress defense is typically precluded. A court could determine that no reasonable juror could find a person of "reasonable firmness" would commit a felony rather than go to the police over a five-day period. Thus, the court could rule as a matter of law that the threat fails to satisfy the legal threshold prior to trial.

Head-to-head: 
Option (a) is the clearly correct application of both the MPC and modern common-law standards. The distractors all utilize strong, absolute language to assert explicitly falsifiable claims. Distractor (b) falsely claims the common law strictly limits threats to the defendant personally, completely ignoring the widely recognized "close relation" rule. Distractor (c) contains the highly falsifiable assertion that the MPC "ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement," which flatly contradicts the black-letter text of MPC § 2.09. Distractor (d) invents an absolute requirement for an "immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" and insists severe beatings "cannot ever qualify." Distractor (e) falsely transforms the fact-intensive "reasonable firmness" inquiry (and the common-law escape element) into an automatic threshold failure "as a strict matter of law prior to trial," contradicting the jury-question nature of the MPC standard. All distractors are cleanly falsifiable and locked with absolute phrasing.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law explicitly extends the target scope to include a defendant's close relatives (such as a mother).
- (c): "the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 deliberately abandons the strict common-law imminence requirement in favor of the "reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" and "cannot ever qualify" — wrong because the common law explicitly recognizes threats of serious bodily injury (such as severe beatings requiring surgery), not just immediate deadly force.
- (e): "immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial" — wrong because under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test in the face of a delay is generally a factual question for the jury, not a strict pre-trial legal threshold failure.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) accurately applies the black-letter rules for duress under both the common law and the Model Penal Code. Under the common law, the threat must be of death or serious bodily injury, and a week-long hospital stay inflicted by a known violent enforcer clearly qualifies as serious bodily harm. Furthermore, the common law traditionally recognizes threats against a "close relation," such as a parent. Finally, the MPC's broader standard requires only a threat of "unlawful force" against "any person," which is easily satisfied by Bentham's threats against both Kant and his mother.

(b) Argument-for: Option (b) could be argued as correct by appealing to the strictest historical common-law formulations of duress, which demanded that the threat of death or great bodily harm be directed at the defendant himself. A student could argue that extending the scope to third parties is a modern MPC innovation rather than a universal common-law rule. By this logic, the coercive pressure from a threat against Kant's mother fails the target scope element. Therefore, any reliance on the threat to the mother is legally insufficient under an orthodox strict reading of the common law.

(c) Argument-for: Option (c) draws upon the fact that the gap between March 10 and March 15 strongly implicates imminence and the opportunity to escape. A student could argue that while the MPC alters the formulation of the defense, it effectively retains the imminence requirement through the "person of reasonable firmness" standard, as no reasonable person would yield to a non-imminent threat without seeking help. Because Bentham promised retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force, the threat lacked immediacy. Thus, the threshold fails under the MPC because the danger was not an unavoidable present danger.

(d) Argument-for: Option (d) asserts a rigid interpretation of the common-law threshold for duress, which was historically reserved for extreme, immediate threats of death. A student might argue that a mere verbal threat of a beating—even a severe one by an enforcer—does not rise to the level of death or the kind of catastrophic bodily harm traditionally required, such as a literal gun to the head. Because the common law strongly disfavors the duress defense, this strict threshold ensures that only the most undeniably overpowering threats qualify. Therefore, Bentham's threat falls short of the common-law standard but satisfies the MPC's "unlawful force" standard.

(e) Argument-for: Option (e) focuses on the five-day gap between the threat and the crime, arguing that this delay defeats the defense under both standards as a strict matter of law. Under the common law, a five-day gap provides ample reasonable opportunity to escape or report to the police, which operates as an absolute bar. Under the MPC, a person of "reasonable firmness" would undoubtedly report a five-day advance threat to authorities rather than commit a bank robbery. Because the facts establish a five-day notice period, a judge would preclude the defense before it ever reaches the jury, making both thresholds fail strictly as a matter of law.

Head-to-head: Option (a) accurately aligns the facts with the standard elements for duress under both the common law and the MPC. Option (b) invents a falsifiable claim that the common law "strictly limits" cognizable threats to the defendant personally, explicitly contradicting the accepted common-law inclusion of "close relations." Option (c) fails because it asserts the MPC "ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement," which is a direct misstatement of MPC § 2.09. Option (d) uses absolutist phrasing ("strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario") to falsely claim that severe beatings cannot satisfy the common-law serious bodily injury threshold. Option (e) falsely collapses the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element into the threat threshold and incorrectly asserts that a five-day delay automatically precludes the MPC defense "as a strict matter of law prior to trial," stripping the jury of its fact-finding role on "reasonable firmness." All distractors are firmly locked with explicit false legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly recognizes threats directed at "close relations" such as parents, spouses, and children.
- (c): "the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because the MPC deliberately abandons the strict imminence requirement in favor of a general "person of reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating... cannot ever qualify" — wrong because the common law explicitly accepts threats of "serious bodily injury" or "grievous bodily harm," not just threats of death or gun-to-the-head scenarios.
- (e): "immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial" — wrong because time delay relates to the distinct "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element at common law (not the threat threshold), and presents a factual jury question regarding "reasonable firmness" under the MPC rather than an automatic legal bar prior to trial.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q13

### Grounding

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative, universally excusing their involvement in any resulting homicide.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks strictly enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense, treating the preservation of innocent life as an unwaivable legal duty.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was entirely unaware their co-felon was armed during the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, however, the defense of duress is categorically barred as an excuse for any murder charge.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant. However, Kant may successfully assert the necessity defense because avoiding serious physical injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than the unfortunate death of the victim, Bayes.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress murder bar and felony murder exception, Ch 21, Refinement `felony-murder-duress-exception`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that because duress negates the culpability required for the predicate felony (armed robbery), the resulting felony murder charge must logically fall. If Kant committed no felony due to the threat to his mother, there is no underlying crime to serve as the springboard for felony murder. Furthermore, under modern reformist logic, an overwhelming threat to a relative negates culpability across the board, making the defense logically "universally available" when the elements of duress are met.
(b) Argument-for: A student might point to the historic, black-letter common law rule: "thou shalt not kill to save thyself." This absolute prohibition traditionally extends to felony murder, as the preservation of innocent life is treated as paramount regardless of the defendant's specific role. The student might reasonably assume that the Model Penal Code, despite its progressive elements, retains this bedrock moral intuition, thus enforcing an "absolute bar" against excusing murder.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that exceptions to the felony murder duress bar are extremely narrow. To benefit from the *Reichard* or *McMillan* rationale, one might logically deduce that a defendant must have perfectly clean hands regarding the lethal risk, which requires being "entirely unaware" the co-felon was armed. Additionally, a student might understandably confuse the MPC's necessity restrictions regarding homicide with its duress provisions, concluding it is "categorically barred" there as well.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that while duress is rigidly barred for murder under traditional common law, necessity provides an alternative route. Kant was forced into a choice of evils: severe, guaranteed injury to his mother versus a mere risk of harm during the robbery. If a court evaluates the certainty of the threat against the unintended death of Bayes, a student might argue that necessity theoretically applies because preventing immediate serious injury functions as the "greater good" in a subjective balancing test.
(e) Argument-for: A student would argue that (e) perfectly captures the nuance of modern criminal law splits. The traditional common law strictly barred duress for murder, but *Reichard* and *McMillan* correctly recognized that if the predicate felony is excused for a non-killer participant, the felony murder rule loses its foundation. Meanwhile, MPC § 2.09 famously abolishes the murder exception, leaving the defense up to the "person of reasonable firmness" standard, making this the only legally accurate synthesis.

Option (e) provides the correct, nuanced application of the traditional common law, the modern exception (*Reichard*/*McMillan*), and the Model Penal Code's formulation of duress. The distractors all utilize strong, absolute language to assert explicitly false legal propositions. Option (a) incorrectly claims universal availability under common law, ignoring the historical murder bar. Option (b) incorrectly claims categorical unavailability under the MPC, directly contradicting § 2.09. Option (c) invents an absolute requirement ("only if... entirely unaware") for the modern exception and repeats the MPC error. Option (d) asserts a fatally flawed necessity calculation, explicitly claiming that preventing physical injury is legally weighed as a greater good than an innocent human life, which violates black-letter choice-of-evils doctrine.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law" — wrong because traditional common law imposes a strict bar on duress for all forms of murder.
- (b): "Duress is categorically unavailable... for murder under the Model Penal Code" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 notoriously lacks a categorical homicide carve-out.
- (c): "only if the defendant was entirely unaware their co-felon was armed" — wrong because the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception does not strictly require total ignorance of a weapon, only that the defendant was a non-killer participant whose underlying felony was excused.
- (d): "avoiding serious physical injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than the unfortunate death of the victim, Bayes" — wrong because common law necessity (choice of evils) categorically forbids weighing physical injury as a greater good than an innocent human life.

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q13.** Assume that Kant, threatened by Bentham with serious bodily injury to Kant's mother, satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. How does the defense of duress apply to Kant for felony murder under the common law, and for murder under the Model Penal Code?

(a) Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because the defense automatically applies whenever a defendant faces a threat of serious bodily injury to a relative, universally excusing their involvement in any resulting homicide.
(b) Duress is categorically unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both legal frameworks strictly enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense, treating the preservation of innocent life as an unwaivable legal duty.
(c) Under the common law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception, duress is available for felony murder only if the defendant was entirely unaware their co-felon was armed during the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, however, the defense of duress is categorically barred as an excuse for any murder charge.
(d) Under the traditional common law, duress is barred for felony murder even for a non-killer participant. However, Kant may successfully assert the necessity defense because avoiding serious physical injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than the unfortunate death of the victim, Bayes.
(e) Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for a non-killer participant who satisfies the defense for the predicate felony. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under the traditional common law, the duress defense is barred for all forms of murder, including felony murder. However, the modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception allows duress for felony murder if the defendant is a non-killer participant whose involvement in the predicate felony was excused by duress. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a categorical homicide exception. (a) is incorrect because the traditional common law bars duress for murder. (b) is incorrect because the MPC allows duress as a defense to homicide. (c) is incorrect because the *Reichard* exception does not require ignorance of a co-felon's weapon, and the MPC does not bar duress for murder. (d) is incorrect because necessity (choice of evils) typically excludes homicide at common law, and avoiding physical injury is not legally recognized as a greater good than preserving a human life.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; People v. Reichard, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); McMillan v. State, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might choose this by focusing on the modern trend and the MPC. The MPC allows duress for murder, and jurisdictions adopting the *Reichard* logic allow it for felony murder. If a student overgeneralizes this trend, they could argue that threats to a relative automatically excuse involvement in the predicate felony, thereby "universally" excusing the resulting felony murder.
(b) Argument-for: A student could defend this by strictly applying the traditional common law rule, which originates from Blackstone and absolutely bars duress as a defense to taking an innocent life. Since felony murder is a homicide offense, the traditional bar applies. A student might assume the MPC retained this deeply entrenched moral bright line, rendering duress "categorically unavailable" across the board.
(c) Argument-for: A student might reasonably believe that the *Reichard* exception requires a complete lack of foreseeability regarding the homicide, meaning a defendant who knows their co-felon is armed cannot be excused. Furthermore, since many states that adopted parts of the MPC explicitly added a homicide exception back into their state duress statutes, a student could mistakenly assume this categorical bar is part of the model code itself.
(d) Argument-for: A student could recognize the traditional common law duress bar for felony murder but attempt an end-run using the necessity (choice of evils) defense. They might argue that since Kant was merely a driver and faced the imminent severe injury of his mother, a utilitarian calculus might weigh the certain preservation of his mother over the probabilistic risk of death to a bystander, successfully satisfying necessity.
(e) Argument-for: This option correctly reflects both the common law and the MPC. Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to all murder, including felony murder. The modern *Reichard*/*McMillan* line of cases carved out a specific exception for non-killer participants whose predicate felony is negated by duress. Meanwhile, MPC § 2.09 consciously abandoned the common law murder bar, making duress available for any crime, including murder, provided the reasonable firmness standard is met.

Head-to-head: 
Option (e) is historically and doctrinally precise. The distractors contain clear, falsifiable legal errors locked with absolute phrasing. Option (a) falsely claims the defense is "universally available" under the common law, which ignores the traditional murder bar. Option (b) falsely claims the MPC strictly enforces an absolute bar against duress for homicide, when MPC § 2.09 explicitly removed it. Option (c) invents a requirement that the defendant be "entirely unaware" the co-felon was armed for the *Reichard* exception to apply, and repeats the false claim about the MPC. Option (d) wrongly asserts that necessity recognizes avoiding physical injury as a legally weighed "greater good" than an innocent human life, which violates the traditional common law rule that necessity is generally unavailable for homicide and requires the avoided harm to actually be greater than the caused harm.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "Duress is universally available for felony murder under the common law" — wrong because traditional common law imposes a strict murder bar, and only specific jurisdictions recognize the exception.
- (b): "both legal frameworks strictly enforce an absolute bar against asserting duress for any homicide offense" — wrong because the MPC deliberately omits a homicide exception under § 2.09.
- (c): "only if the defendant was entirely unaware their co-felon was armed" and "Under the Model Penal Code... categorically barred" — wrong because *Reichard* does not demand strict ignorance of a weapon, and the MPC expressly allows duress for murder.
- (d): "avoiding serious physical injury to his mother is legally weighed as a greater good than the unfortunate death of the victim" — wrong because common law necessity does not weigh physical injury as greater than innocent human life, nor does it typically excuse homicide.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q14

### Grounding

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted. Under this strictly subjective view, as long as Kant genuinely feared Bentham's leveled revolver and remembered the hospitalization threat, the jury cannot second-guess his defensive action using an external, objective standard.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry.

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry. Consequently, the jury may consider the distance of six feet and the revolver, but must ignore Bentham's explicit threat.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness. Under this specific framework, Kant's honest fear of the leveled revolver and prior threat satisfies the justification, precluding the jury from assessing his defensive reaction against a hypothetical objective baseline.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold. This test evaluates whether the severe situational pressure of facing a gun from six feet without cover would compel a person to break the law, thereby excusing Kant's lethal response against the aggressor.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Goetz objective standard and situational incorporation, Ch 22, Refinement `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. <!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that *Goetz* is famous for subjectiveizing the self-defense inquiry. The Appellate Division in that case held that the standard was "reasonable to him," and if a student misremembers the Court of Appeals' holding as affirming rather than reversing that specific formulation, they would logically choose this option since Kant possessed a genuine subjective fear.
(b) Argument-for: This accurately tracks the holding of *People v. Goetz*. The New York standard is an objective "reasonable person" test that is contextualized by placing the hypothetical reasonable person in the defendant's exact "situation." This situation explicitly incorporates both the physical realities (six feet, no cover) and the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker (the explicit threat of hospitalization).
(c) Argument-for: A student might recall that *Goetz* reversed the lower courts precisely to save the objective standard. By insisting that self-defense must remain objective, a student might incorrectly reason that subjective factors—like a defendant's idiosyncratic prior experiences or knowledge—must be categorically excluded to prevent the standard from collapsing into subjectivity. Thus, (c) sounds like a rigorous application of an objective test.
(d) Argument-for: New York's penal law was heavily revised during the era of the Model Penal Code. A student might assume New York adopted the MPC's justification provisions wholesale. The MPC self-defense justification relies on whether the actor "believes" force is necessary, without the modifier "reasonably." If a student assumes this MPC rule governs New York, (d) provides the logical, correct doctrinal outcome.
(e) Argument-for: The extreme nature of the threat—a gun leveled at the chest from six feet—creates immense psychological pressure. A student might synthesize this pressure with the "person of reasonable firmness" test, arguing that the law excuses defensive actions where severe situational coercion and the threat of unlawful physical force overcomes standard human resolve.

Head-to-head: The keyed answer (b) perfectly captures the nuance of *Goetz*, recognizing that New York employs an objective standard that incorporates the defendant's specific situation and prior knowledge. All distractors provide falsifiable, strictly worded misstatements of law. Option (a) makes an explicitly false historical/doctrinal claim that *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's test. Option (c) makes an explicit false legal claim that *Goetz* categorically excludes prior experiences. Option (d) contains an explicit false doctrinal claim that New York followed the MPC's purely subjective formulation. Option (e) explicitly and falsely imports the "person of reasonable firmness" standard (which belongs to duress) as the controlling inquiry for self-defense. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" and "strictly subjective view" — wrong because the NY Court of Appeals in *Goetz* expressly rejected the Appellate Division's purely subjective reading and retained an objective reasonable-person component.
- (c): "the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences... must ignore Bentham's explicit threat" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the standard incorporates the defendant's "situation," which legally includes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because New York expressly added the word "reasonably" to its statute specifically to reject the MPC's purely subjective standard, a legislative history specifically noted in *Goetz*.
- (e): "the controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist" — wrong because the "person of reasonable firmness" is the standard for the excuse of duress, not the justification of self-defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted. Under this strictly subjective view, as long as Kant genuinely feared Bentham's leveled revolver and remembered the hospitalization threat, the jury cannot second-guess his defensive action using an external, objective standard.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry.

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry. Consequently, the jury may consider the distance of six feet and the revolver, but must ignore Bentham's explicit threat.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness. Under this specific framework, Kant's honest fear of the leveled revolver and prior threat satisfies the justification, precluding the jury from assessing his defensive reaction against a hypothetical objective baseline.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold. This test evaluates whether the severe situational pressure of facing a gun from six feet without cover would compel a person to break the law, thereby excusing Kant's lethal response against the aggressor.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that *Goetz* effectively created a subjective standard by permitting the jury to consider the defendant's unique background, prior experiences, and physical attributes. Since the standard is highly tied to what the defendant knew and experienced, one could claim it operates as the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" test in practice, prohibiting second-guessing by a truly external objective standard.
(b) Argument-for: This tracks the exact holding of *Goetz*. The New York Court of Appeals established a hybrid standard: the factfinder must determine whether a reasonable person *in the defendant's situation* would have believed deadly force was necessary. Incorporating Kant's specific knowledge of Bentham's hospitalization threat and the physical realities of the confrontation perfectly models this contextualized objective inquiry.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that the very definition of an "objective" standard requires stripping away the defendant's personal idiosyncrasies and unique knowledge. To prevent the standard from collapsing into subjectivity, a strict objective test must rely only on external facts—the revolver and the distance—while excluding internal elements like Kant's memory of prior threats.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that *Goetz* distinguished New York law from the Model Penal Code and mistakenly conclude that New York *adopted* the MPC's purely subjective approach. If New York followed the MPC, the inquiry would end at Kant's honest belief that deadly force was necessary, making his internal fear sufficient to justify the shooting without an objective baseline.
(e) Argument-for: A student might conflate the justification of self-defense with the excuse of duress. Under the MPC's duress standard, the key metric is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist the pressure. Facing a leveled revolver at close range is an immense situational pressure, which could plausibly trigger this standard if mistakenly applied to self-defense.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly states the legal standard from *Goetz*, which requires an objective assessment situated in the defendant's precise circumstances, including prior knowledge. Option (a) makes an explicit, false historical and doctrinal claim by asserting *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's strictly subjective standard, which the Court of Appeals specifically reversed. Option (e) explicitly imports the "person of reasonable firmness" test, which is exclusively an element of duress, not self-defense. All distractors are definitively wrong.

- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because the Court of Appeals explicitly reversed the Appellate Division and rejected this purely subjective test.
- (c): "the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences" — wrong because *Goetz* expressly requires the jury to consider the defendant's prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because New York's legislature purposefully added "reasonably," thereby rejecting the MPC's purely subjective approach.
- (e): "the controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist firing" — wrong because this is the standard for duress, not the self-defense standard for the reasonableness of deadly force.

Recommended fix: none

## Q15

### Grounding

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. How would the duty-to-retreat analysis apply to Kant?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor. Because his receipt of stolen proceeds does not legally disqualify him, the fifteen-foot exit route has no bearing on his self-defense claim here.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard. The fact that Bentham has leveled a revolver from six feet away and Kant lacks cover is considered legally irrelevant to this analysis.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse. In such non-residential environments, the duty to retreat is reinstated, meaning Kant must attempt the fifteen-foot dash to the exit despite the leveled revolver and lack of cover.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant likely has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete safety; in Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is engaged in criminal activity; in Jurisdiction C, Kant has no duty to retreat.
(e) Kant is exempt from retreating in all three jurisdictions because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception regardless of other statutory limits. The fact that he is engaged in criminal activity or facing a leveled revolver does not negate his fundamental right to stand his ground.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly applies each jurisdiction's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; running fifteen feet across an open floor against a leveled revolver fails that standard. In Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is "engaged in criminal activity," as stipulated in the prompt. Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (a) fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as sufficient. (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which explicitly applies in "any setting." (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule that overrides the specific jurisdictional rules provided.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duty to Retreat and Stand Your Ground, Ch 22, Refinement `duty-to-retreat-general`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. How would the duty-to-retreat analysis apply to Kant?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor. Because his receipt of stolen proceeds does not legally disqualify him, the fifteen-foot exit route has no bearing on his self-defense claim here.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard. The fact that Bentham has leveled a revolver from six feet away and Kant lacks cover is considered legally irrelevant to this analysis.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse. In such non-residential environments, the duty to retreat is reinstated, meaning Kant must attempt the fifteen-foot dash to the exit despite the leveled revolver and lack of cover.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant likely has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete safety; in Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is engaged in criminal activity; in Jurisdiction C, Kant has no duty to retreat.
(e) Kant is exempt from retreating in all three jurisdictions because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception regardless of other statutory limits. The fact that he is engaged in criminal activity or facing a leveled revolver does not negate his fundamental right to stand his ground.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly applies each jurisdiction's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; running fifteen feet across an open floor against a leveled revolver fails that standard. In Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is "engaged in criminal activity," as stipulated in the prompt. Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (a) fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as sufficient. (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which explicitly applies in "any setting." (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule that overrides the specific jurisdictional rules provided.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

### Edge Case Audit

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. How would the duty-to-retreat analysis apply to Kant?

(a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor. Because his receipt of stolen proceeds does not legally disqualify him, the fifteen-foot exit route has no bearing on his self-defense claim here.
(b) In Jurisdiction A, Kant must retreat because the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes a physically available escape route, which automatically satisfies the retreat standard. The fact that Bentham has leveled a revolver from six feet away and Kant lacks cover is considered legally irrelevant to this analysis.
(c) In Jurisdiction C, Kant must retreat because the jurisdiction's absolute no-retreat rule does not apply to commercial structures like a warehouse. In such non-residential environments, the duty to retreat is reinstated, meaning Kant must attempt the fifteen-foot dash to the exit despite the leveled revolver and lack of cover.
(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant likely has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete safety; in Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is engaged in criminal activity; in Jurisdiction C, Kant has no duty to retreat.
(e) Kant is exempt from retreating in all three jurisdictions because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception regardless of other statutory limits. The fact that he is engaged in criminal activity or facing a leveled revolver does not negate his fundamental right to stand his ground.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly applies each jurisdiction's provided rule. Jurisdiction A requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; running fifteen feet across an open floor against a leveled revolver fails that standard. In Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is "engaged in criminal activity," as stipulated in the prompt. Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (a) fabricates a limitation to Jurisdiction B's categorical "criminal activity" bar. (b) ignores Jurisdiction A's "complete personal safety" requirement, treating mere physical availability as sufficient. (c) misapplies Jurisdiction C's rule, which explicitly applies in "any setting." (e) relies on a fabricated universal workplace castle-doctrine rule that overrides the specific jurisdictional rules provided.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (Duty to retreat limitations; "complete personal safety" standard; "engaged in criminal activity" SYG exclusions).

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity." Jurisdiction A requires retreat before using deadly force, but only if it can be accomplished "with complete personal safety." Jurisdiction B's Stand Your Ground statute imposes no duty to retreat unless the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity." Jurisdiction C imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (a) In Jurisdiction B, Kant maintains his right to stand his ground because the "criminal activity" limitation applies only to crimes perpetrated against the initial aggressor. Because his receipt of stolen proceeds does not legally disqualify him, the fifteen-foot exit route has no bearing on his self-defense claim here.

The fact that Bentham has leveled a revolver from six feet away and Kant lacks cover is considered legally irrelevant to this analysis.

(d) In Jurisdiction A, Kant likely has no duty to retreat because a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver cannot be completed with complete safety; in Jurisdiction B, Kant loses the right to stand his ground because he is engaged in criminal activity; in Jurisdiction C, Kant has no duty to retreat.
(e) Kant is exempt from retreating in all three jurisdictions because the warehouse constitutes a workplace, which universally triggers the castle doctrine exception regardless of other statutory limits. The fact that he is engaged in criminal activity or facing a leveled revolver does not negate his fundamental right to stand his ground.

---

## Q16

### Grounding

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty.

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder, rendering the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity completely irrelevant to a homeowner's absolute privilege to defend their personal sanctuary from any outside breach.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat, meaning Kant is legally presumed to have reasonably feared death or great bodily harm regardless of whether Officer Hand was lawfully executing a validly issued arrest warrant following proper loudspeaker announcements at the apartment door.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations, legally stripping him of the right to assert a self-defense claim or invoke the protection of his dwelling even against an unrelated third party like Officer Hand who approaches him later in a separate location to execute a lawful arrest warrant.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home, thus allowing residents to use physical force against police officers carrying out court orders so long as the confrontation takes place entirely within the physical boundaries of the resident's dwelling place rather than out on the public street.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Castle Doctrine, Ch 22, Refinement `castle-doctrine-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->
