**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN

(a) Argument-for: This option accurately reflects the intersection between the castle doctrine and the foundational elements of self-defense. The castle doctrine operates solely as an exception to the duty to retreat, not as a replacement for the primary requirement that the victim must be facing unlawful force. Because Officer Hand was executing a valid arrest warrant after proper identification, his entry was legally authorized, meaning Kant faced no "unlawful force" to justify self-defense. Thus, the predicate fails regardless of the retreat rules.

(b) Argument-for: A student might select this option by taking Cardozo's rhetoric in *People v. Tomlins* to its absolute, literal extreme. The argument would posit that if a man's home is truly his "castle," his privilege to repel physical breaches of its threshold must be absolute and blind to the intruder's identity or legal authority. Under this strict textual reading of the "sanctuary" concept, any uninvited forced entry triggers an absolute right to use force, overriding the specific mechanics of lawful warrants.

(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that modern statutory castle doctrines, such as Florida's "Stand Your Ground" laws, are designed to aggressively shield homeowners by establishing legal presumptions that override common-law self-defense analysis. This argument asserts that the statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies mechanically to any forced entry, dispensing with the need for courts to evaluate whether the entrant's force was technically "unlawful" or whether the entrant was a police officer.

(d) Argument-for: This argument relies on a strict, continuous-transaction theory of the initial-aggressor doctrine. If Kant was the initial aggressor in an earlier, closely related encounter and never properly withdrew or communicated his withdrawal, a student could argue that this disqualifying status "attaches" to him as he flees. Under this theory, his continuous flight prevents him from regaining the right to self-defense, even when confronted by an unrelated party (the police) at his final destination.

(e) Argument-for: A student might choose this option if they believe the castle doctrine is a universally uniform constitutional or common-law principle across the United States. The argument assumes that the sanctity of the home is so paramount that no jurisdiction allows police to forcefully enter without risking legally justified violent resistance, effectively asserting that "lawful entrant" exceptions do not exist when the physical boundary of a dwelling is breached.

Head-to-head:
Option (a) correctly grounds the legal outcome in the foundational requirement of "unlawful force" for any self-defense claim, properly recognizing that the castle doctrine only modifies the duty to retreat. Options (b), (c), (d), and (e) are structurally excellent distractors because they each boldly state a completely false, absolutist proposition about the boundaries of the castle doctrine, statutory presumptions, or initial-aggressor rules. Each distractor uses explicitly falsifiable language ("without exception", "regardless of whether", "across all subsequent confrontations", "in every U.S. jurisdiction") that strictly satisfies the close-call standard. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder, rendering the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity completely irrelevant" — wrong because self-defense fundamentally requires the threat to be unlawful; a lawful police entry under a valid warrant defeats the self-defense predicate, so it is highly relevant.
- (c): "dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat... regardless of whether Officer Hand was lawfully executing a validly issued arrest warrant" — wrong because statutory presumptions of reasonable fear explicitly exclude, or do not apply against, law enforcement officers lawfully executing their duties.
- (d): "follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations... even against an unrelated third party" — wrong because the initial-aggressor bar is specific to the discrete confrontation and the specific victim, not a continuous, portable disability against unrelated third parties.
- (e): "applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception" — wrong because every U.S. jurisdiction recognizes that executing a valid warrant is lawful force and therefore cannot be the basis for a self-defense claim.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q16.** Assume that, whether or not Kant prevails on any other defense, he used defensive force against Officer Hand inside his Elm Street apartment after Officer Hand's lawful forced entry under a valid arrest warrant. Evaluate whether the castle doctrine authorizes Kant's defensive force against Officer Hand.

(a) The castle doctrine eliminates any duty to retreat from one's home but does not transform otherwise unjustified force into lawful self-defense; because Officer Hand was a lawful entrant executing a valid warrant after loudspeaker identification, his entry was not "unlawful force," and the predicate for any self-defense claim fails regardless of the castle doctrine's modification of the retreat duty.

(b) The castle doctrine authorizes defensive force against any person entering the home, including law-enforcement officers executing a warrant, because Cardozo's formulation in *People v. Tomlins* protects the dwelling without exception as to the identity of the intruder, rendering the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity completely irrelevant to a homeowner's absolute privilege to defend their personal sanctuary from any outside breach.

(c) The castle doctrine permits defensive force here because Florida's statutory presumption of reasonable fear applies to any in-home force claim and dispenses with the need to show that the entrant posed an actual imminent unlawful threat, meaning Kant is legally presumed to have reasonably feared death or great bodily harm regardless of whether Officer Hand was lawfully executing a validly issued arrest warrant following proper loudspeaker announcements at the apartment door.

(d) The castle doctrine is unavailable because Kant was the initial aggressor in the warehouse encounter with Bentham, and the initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations, legally stripping him of the right to assert a self-defense claim or invoke the protection of his dwelling even against an unrelated third party like Officer Hand who approaches him later in a separate location to execute a lawful arrest warrant.

(e) The castle doctrine applies in every U.S. jurisdiction to permit defensive force against a person executing a warrant, because no state recognizes a "lawful entrant" exception to the sanctuary protection of the home, thus allowing residents to use physical force against police officers carrying out court orders so long as the confrontation takes place entirely within the physical boundaries of the resident's dwelling place rather than out on the public street.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly identifies the castle doctrine's structural limit. The doctrine eliminates a duty to retreat from one's home, but self-defense still requires that the aggressor be using "unlawful" force. Officer Hand's entry under a valid warrant, preceded by loudspeaker identification, is lawful by definition (Facts 11, 14). Without unlawful force, there is no self-defense predicate for the castle doctrine to modify. Whether Kant has any other defense (insanity, imperfect self-defense) is taken up separately in Q7–Q9. (b) misreads *Tomlins*, which addressed the duty to retreat, not the self-defense predicate of unlawful force. (c) falsely describes FL's statutory presumption; the presumption applies to unlawful or forcible entry, and entry under a valid warrant is lawful. (d) falsely carries initial-aggressor status across unrelated encounters; the initial-aggressor bar applies within a discrete confrontation, not as a life-long disability. (e) falsely universalizes castle-doctrine protection against lawful entrants; every jurisdiction treats lawful police entry as non-predicate for self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [castle-doctrine, lawful-entrant, initial-aggressor, unlawful-force-predicate], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I (*People v. Tomlins*, 213 N.Y. 240 (1914): castle doctrine eliminates retreat duty but requires underlying unlawful force).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly isolates the foundational structure of self-defense claims. The castle doctrine merely operates as an exception to the duty to retreat; it does not alter the absolute requirement that a defendant must be facing an imminent threat of *unlawful* force. Because Officer Hand was executing a valid arrest warrant following proper announcements, his entry was legally justified, meaning the indispensable "unlawful force" predicate for self-defense is absent and the castle doctrine cannot be triggered.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that the historical underpinning of the castle doctrine, as articulated in cases like *People v. Tomlins*, places supreme value on the home as an inviolable sanctuary. Under a broad interpretation of this absolute sanctuary principle, the homeowner's right to defend against an intrusion is paramount, rendering the identity of the entrant and the legal nuances of warrant execution secondary to the absolute privilege of territorial defense against forced breaches.
(c) Argument-for: Florida's Stand Your Ground law establishes a powerful statutory presumption of reasonable fear of death or great bodily harm when someone enters a dwelling. A student might argue that this presumption is robust enough to operate mechanically upon any entry, essentially overriding common-law predicates. Under this textualist view, the mere fact of an intrusion into the home automatically triggers the presumption of reasonable fear, allowing a self-defense claim regardless of the entrant's law-enforcement status.
(d) Argument-for: The initial-aggressor rule conventionally strips a defendant of the right to claim self-defense. A student could argue that because Kant's initial aggression against Bentham directly caused the subsequent police pursuit and confrontation, his initial-aggressor status fundamentally taints his legal standing. Therefore, the disability follows him through the continuous chain of events, categorically barring him from claiming self-defense or invoking the castle doctrine against the officers arriving to arrest him for that very aggression.
(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that the essence of the castle doctrine relies strictly on the physical boundaries of the home. This option suggests that the sanctity of the threshold categorically permits defensive force against any uninvited entrant in every jurisdiction, effectively arguing that property boundaries override statutory law-enforcement privileges and that there is no explicit caveat for lawful entrants once the threshold of the home is breached.

Head-to-head:
Option (a) correctly grounds the analysis in the universally required predicate of unlawful force. The distractors all utilize absolute, definitively false legal claims. Option (b) wrongly asserts that *Tomlins* creates a privilege "without exception" that renders the legality of the entry "completely irrelevant." Option (c) falsely claims Florida's statutory presumption applies to "any" in-home force claim "regardless" of lawful warrant execution. Option (d) fabricates a rule that initial-aggressor status explicitly follows a defendant "across all subsequent confrontations" and against an "unrelated third party." Option (e) relies on an easily falsifiable universal claim that "no state" recognizes a lawful entrant exception and that the doctrine applies "in every U.S. jurisdiction" to permit force against police executing valid court orders.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "rendering the legality of the officer's initial entry and the warrant's validity completely irrelevant" — wrong because self-defense inherently requires a predicate of unlawful force; the legality of the entry completely dictates whether self-defense is legally available.
- (c): "applies to any in-home force claim... regardless of whether Officer Hand was lawfully executing a validly issued arrest warrant" — wrong because statutory presumptions of reasonable fear explicitly exempt the lawful actions of law enforcement officers executing their duties.
- (d): "initial-aggressor bar follows the defendant across all subsequent confrontations... even against an unrelated third party" — wrong because the initial-aggressor doctrine applies to specific, discrete confrontations, not as a continuous, transferable legal disability against unrelated third parties in new locations.
- (e): "no state recognizes a 'lawful entrant' exception... allowing residents to use physical force against police officers carrying out court orders" — wrong because all jurisdictions require unlawful force for self-defense, meaning lawful police entry inherently forecloses the right to defensive force.

Recommended fix: none

## Q17

### Grounding

**Q17.** Assume that, rather than experiencing a psychotic delusion, Kant is charged with attempted murder after stabbing Officer Hand because he genuinely but unreasonably mistook Hand's radio for a drawn firearm and believed Hand was about to unlawfully shoot him. Evaluate Kant's eligibility for imperfect self-defense under the California approach (which recognizes that a genuinely held but unreasonable belief negates malice) versus the traditional New York approach (which treats self-defense as an all-or-nothing justification and rejects imperfect self-defense).

(a) Under the California approach, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because his genuine belief in the need for self-defense negates malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because his unreasonable mistake of fact defeats the justification.

(b) Under the California approach, Kant is ineligible because a belief based on an objectively unreasonable mistake of fact cannot negate malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because the state treats self-defense strictly as an all-or-nothing justification.

(c) Under both approaches, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because both jurisdictions recognize that any genuinely held defensive belief, even if based on an unreasonable mistake of fact, negates the specific intent and malice required for an attempted murder conviction.

(d) Under the California approach, imperfect self-defense mitigates the attempted murder charge to attempted criminally negligent homicide; under the New York approach, his genuine but unreasonable belief serves as a complete justification and mandates a full acquittal of the charges.

(e) The Supreme Court's decision in *Kahler v. Kansas* requires every state to recognize imperfect self-defense under the Due Process Clause, meaning both the California and New York approaches are constitutionally required to mitigate Kant's charge down to a lesser-included offense.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) is correct because under California law, a genuinely held but objectively unreasonable belief in the need for self-defense negates malice and mitigates attempted murder to attempted voluntary manslaughter. Under the traditional New York approach, an unreasonable belief defeats self-defense entirely, resulting in no mitigation or justification. (b) misapplies the provided California rule, which explicitly allows an unreasonable but genuine mistake of fact to negate malice. (c) misapplies the provided New York rule, which rejects the doctrine. (d) misidentifies the grading effect in California (it mitigates to attempted voluntary manslaughter, not criminally negligent homicide) and misapplies the New York rule. (e) misreads *Kahler v. Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses and does not mandate imperfect self-defense.

**Tags:** chapters: [22, 23], topics: [imperfect-self-defense, Schuller-genuineness, jurisdictional-variation, Kahler], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part III (*People v. Schuller*, 15 Cal.5th 237 (2023): genuineness-only rule for imperfect self-defense; Part I table: NY rejects imperfect self-defense); Chapter 23 (*Kahler v. Kansas*, 589 U.S. 271 (2020): due-process deference to state choice).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Imperfect Self-Defense, Ch 22, Refinement `imperfect-self-defense` (and Ch 23 `kahler-constitutional-floor`). Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

**Q17.** Assume that, rather than experiencing a psychotic delusion, Kant is charged with attempted murder after stabbing Officer Hand because he genuinely but unreasonably mistook Hand's radio for a drawn firearm and believed Hand was about to unlawfully shoot him. (a) Under the California approach, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because his genuine belief in the need for self-defense negates malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because his unreasonable mistake of fact defeats the justification.

(b) Under the California approach, Kant is ineligible because a belief based on an objectively unreasonable mistake of fact cannot negate malice; under the New York approach, his claim fails entirely because the state treats self-defense strictly as an all-or-nothing justification.

(c) Under both approaches, Kant is eligible for imperfect self-defense because both jurisdictions recognize that any genuinely held defensive belief, even if based on an unreasonable mistake of fact, negates the specific intent and malice required for an attempted murder conviction.

(d) Under the California approach, imperfect self-defense mitigates the attempted murder charge to attempted criminally negligent homicide; under the New York approach, his genuine but unreasonable belief serves as a complete justification and mandates a full acquittal of the charges.

Kansas*, which held that due process imposes no specific baseline for mental-state defenses and does not mandate imperfect self-defense.

Meanwhile, New York adheres to the traditional rule where self-defense is all-or-nothing, meaning an unreasonable mistake of fact entirely defeats the justification and provides no mitigation.

- (b): "Kant is ineligible because a belief based on an objectively unreasonable mistake of fact cannot negate malice" — wrong because it directly contradicts California law (and the prompt), which uses this exact scenario to negate malice.

## Q18

### Grounding

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions; Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer, coupled with Dr. Singer's testimony about his awareness of the penal code, conclusively demonstrates his knowledge that the physical stabbing was an illegal act under the law.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his psychotic delusion fundamentally distorted his perception of Officer Hand as a demon rather than a human; under the M'Naghten standard, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; a diagnosis of paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that requisite showing of future dangerousness to society, is structurally insufficient to meet the strict federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to a directed verdict of acquittal because the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a baseline floor for insanity defenses; states are not permitted to abolish the moral capacity inquiry when evaluating severe psychotic episodes.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to destroy the demon; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — M'Naghten wrongness, Ch 23, Refinement `mnaghten-wrongness`. Rule paraphrased accurately. Distractors are specifically supported by `mnaghten-nature-quality`, `federal-idra-requirements`, and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions; Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer, coupled with Dr. Singer's testimony about his awareness of the penal code, conclusively demonstrates his knowledge that the physical stabbing was an illegal act under the law.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his psychotic delusion fundamentally distorted his perception of Officer Hand as a demon rather than a human; under the M'Naghten standard, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; a diagnosis of paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that requisite showing of future dangerousness to society, is structurally insufficient to meet the strict federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to a directed verdict of acquittal because the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a baseline floor for insanity defenses; states are not permitted to abolish the moral capacity inquiry when evaluating severe psychotic episodes.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to destroy the demon; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

(a) Argument-for: Some jurisdictions define "wrongness" strictly as legal wrongness, and students might argue that the original English M'Naghten case itself referred to the law of the land. Under this view, Kant's subjective moral delusion is legally irrelevant, and his knowledge of the penal code uniformly dooms his defense under a strict black-letter interpretation.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that stabbing a demon is physically and fundamentally a different act than stabbing a human, meaning Kant didn't understand the physical nature and quality of his act. (e) Argument-for: This option perfectly states the dual interpretations of M'Naghten's wrongness prong. Under the moral-wrong standard, Kant's delusion provides a defense because he believed his actions were morally commanded; under the legal-wrong standard, his awareness that stabbing the officer violated the penal code defeats the defense.

The distractors all feature strongly worded, explicitly falsifiable claims about doctrine or Supreme Court holdings. Option (a) incorrectly claims the standard is uniform. Option (b) explicitly redefines the cognitive nature-and-quality prong as a moral test. Option (c) inserts a non-existent element into the federal IDRA statute. Option (d) states the exact opposite of the holding in *Kahler v. Kansas*.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions" — wrong because there is a well-known jurisdictional split between legal wrongness and moral wrongness.

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q18.** Assume that at the time of the stabbing, Kant was suffering from a qualifying mental disease (paranoid schizophrenia) in an active psychotic episode. As established by Dr. Singer's testimony, Kant specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was a crime under the penal code, but his delusion convinced him he was morally required to sacrifice the human host to destroy a demon possessing Officer Hand's body. Apply the M'Naghten wrongness prong to Kant's conduct, distinguishing jurisdictions that define wrongness morally from those that define it legally.

(a) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the wrongness prong uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions; Kant's violent resistance to a uniformed officer, coupled with Dr. Singer's testimony about his awareness of the penal code, conclusively demonstrates his knowledge that the physical stabbing was an illegal act under the law.
(b) Kant satisfies M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong because his psychotic delusion fundamentally distorted his perception of Officer Hand as a demon rather than a human; under the M'Naghten standard, the nature-and-quality prong categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
(c) Kant fails the M'Naghten test because the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public; a diagnosis of paranoid schizophrenia alone, without that requisite showing of future dangerousness to society, is structurally insufficient to meet the strict federal standard.
(d) Kant is constitutionally entitled to a directed verdict of acquittal because the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong as a baseline floor for insanity defenses; states are not permitted to abolish the moral capacity inquiry when evaluating severe psychotic episodes.
(e) Kant satisfies the wrongness prong under M'Naghten's moral-wrongfulness formulation because his delusion rendered him unable to recognize the stabbing as morally wrong, believing it was morally required to destroy the demon; in jurisdictions that limit wrongness to legal wrongfulness, Kant fails the prong because he understood that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) correctly applies M'Naghten's wrongness prong based on the recognized jurisdictional split. Under a moral formulation, Kant's delusion provided a subjective moral justification, satisfying the prong. Under a legal formulation, he fails the prong because he specifically knew that stabbing Officer Hand was legally prohibited. (a) falsely claims the wrongness prong uniformly demands legal wrongfulness, ignoring the split. (b) states a categorically false legal rule; the "nature and quality" prong evaluates the physical nature of the act (e.g., physically stabbing a living human versus squeezing a grapefruit), not the moral understanding of the victim's identity. (c) fabricates a "specific danger to the public" element for the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act. (d) inverts *Kahler v. Kansas*, which explicitly held that due process does not require states to adopt the M'Naghten wrongness test.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [M'Naghten, wrongness-prong, moral-vs-legal-wrongfulness, nature-and-quality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Part A (M'Naghten's two prongs); Part D (post-Hinckley reforms); Kahler v. Kansas, 589 U.S. 271 (2020) (cataloguing cognitive/moral strains).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that if an individual knows an act is illegal, they possess the minimum threshold of cognitive awareness to be held culpable in most modern legal contexts. Drawing on rigid interpretations of the M'Naghten rule in jurisdictions that explicitly reject moral delusions, the student might conclude that a known violation of the penal code is the uniform baseline for failing the insanity defense's cognitive tests.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that perceiving a person as a demon inherently alters the "nature" of the act, as destroying a supernatural entity is fundamentally different from killing a human being. The argument would assert that "nature and quality" extends beyond mere physics (the physical mechanics of stabbing) to the cognitive categorization of the object being acted upon, meaning a failure to recognize the victim as a human satisfies this prong.
(c) Argument-for: The federal Insanity Defense Reform Act severely narrowed the insanity defense, shifting the burden to the defendant and requiring a "severe" mental disease. A student could argue that because successful federal insanity acquittees face commitment hearings where dangerousness is assessed (18 U.S.C. § 4243), the element of public protection and future dangerousness is structurally intertwined with the strict federal insanity framework.
(d) Argument-for: A student might mistakenly recall *Kahler v. Kansas* as a case that established constitutional baselines for fundamental fairness in criminal law. The argument would assert that the principle of moral culpability is so deeply rooted in Anglo-American history that abolishing the wrongness prong offends substantive due process, making an insanity defense based on moral capacity a constitutional requirement.
(e) Argument-for: This applies the established jurisdictional split regarding M'Naghten's wrongness prong. In jurisdictions defining "wrong" morally, Kant's delusion that the stabbing was morally necessary to save the host from a demon negates his appreciation of wrongfulness. In states strictly defining "wrong" as legally wrong, Kant's knowledge that his conduct violated the penal code prevents him from meeting the standard.

Head-to-head:
Option (e) accurately captures the doctrine and the specific jurisdictional split requested in the prompt. Distractor (a) contains the absolute claim that wrongness uniformly requires legal wrongness across all jurisdictions, which is directly falsified by the existence of the moral-wrongness standard. Distractor (b) explicitly excludes "baseline awareness of the physical mechanics of the act" from the nature-and-quality prong, which contradicts the classic definition of that prong (e.g., strangling a person vs. squeezing a lemon). Distractor (c) incorporates future dangerousness as an element of the insanity defense under IDRA, which is statutorily false. Distractor (d) makes an explicitly false claim about the holding of *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled the exact opposite. Because every distractor contains an explicit, objectively false legal proposition, the question is highly rigorous and requires no edits.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "uniformly requires proof that the defendant did not know the act was legally prohibited across all jurisdictions" — wrong because there is a well-known split where many jurisdictions define wrongness morally.
- (b): "categorically evaluates the defendant's moral understanding of the victim's true identity rather than their baseline awareness of the physical mechanics" — wrong because the nature and quality prong specifically and primarily evaluates the defendant's awareness of the physical mechanics of the act.
- (c): "requires a 'severe' mental disease combined with clear-and-convincing-evidence proof of a specific danger to the public" — wrong because the federal insanity defense under 18 U.S.C. § 17 does not include proof of future danger to the public as an element of the defense.
- (d): "Kahler v. Kansas held that the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause strictly requires every state to recognize M'Naghten's wrongness prong" — wrong because Kahler held exactly the opposite, explicitly permitting states to abolish the wrongness prong.

## Q19

### Grounding

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of whether his cognitive appreciation of wrongfulness was merely substantially impaired rather than totally absent.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable.
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate in order to successfully establish an affirmative defense of insanity at trial.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will successfully establish an insanity defense in either federal or state court without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" or "beyond a reasonable doubt" threshold.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior rather than their cognitive intent—is therefore no longer legally available or permissible to assert in any federal or state jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs. The Insanity Defense Reform Act tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC vs. IDRA insanity tests, Ch 23, Refinement `federal-idra-requirements`, `mpc-conform-conduct`, `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`, and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Edge Case Audit

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." (b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law—and need satisfy only one. (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of whether his cognitive appreciation of wrongfulness was merely substantially impaired rather than totally absent.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable.
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate in order to successfully establish an affirmative defense of insanity at trial.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will successfully establish an insanity defense in either federal or state court without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" or "beyond a reasonable doubt" threshold.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior rather than their cognitive intent—is therefore no longer legally available or permissible to assert in any federal or state jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs. The Insanity Defense Reform Act tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under a misunderstanding of legislative history, one might argue that the IDRA codified the clinical realities of psychiatric defenses by embracing the broader MPC. If one assumes the federal statute incorporated the MPC's volitional prong to match modern psychiatric standards, then Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct" would perfectly align with that rule. A student might conclude this guarantees victory in federal court.
(b) Argument-for: The Model Penal Code § 4.01 establishes a disjunctive standard ("lacks substantial capacity either to appreciate the criminality [wrongfulness] of his conduct or to conform his conduct to the requirements of law"). Dr. Laplace's findings directly satisfy both prongs. Conversely, the 1984 IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) eliminated the volitional prong, required the mental disease to be "severe," demanded absolute inability to appreciate wrongfulness, and shifted the burden to clear and convincing evidence. Option (b) correctly synthesizes all doctrinal elements of both standards.
(c) Argument-for: A student could strictly interpret the word "unable" as the absolute baseline for all legal insanity defenses, mistakenly projecting M'Naghten's historical strictness or the IDRA's rigid language onto the MPC. Under this view, "substantial impairment" might be dismissed as mere diminished capacity. An advocate could argue that a true insanity defense intrinsically requires total incapacity, making Dr. Laplace's "substantial impairment" finding legally insufficient even under the MPC.
(d) Argument-for: Many affirmative defenses historically place the burden of proof on the defendant by a preponderance of the evidence. One could argue that the IDRA and MPC establish a uniform evidentiary baseline of preponderance for affirmative defenses to avoid constitutional due process issues. Under this premise, Laplace's uncontested testimony would easily clear the preponderance bar in both state and federal jurisdictions, leading logically to the conclusion in (d).
(e) Argument-for: In *Kahler v. Kansas*, the Supreme Court analyzed the constitutionality of a state limiting the traditional insanity defense. An advocate could aggressively interpret *Kahler* as holding that because a state *can* limit the defense to the negation of mens rea, any broader affirmative defense (like the volitional prong) violates fundamental constitutional principles of criminal responsibility. Under this expansive reading, the volitional prong is unconstitutional and uniformly impermissible.

Head-to-head: Option (b) provides a flawlessly accurate comparison of the MPC and the IDRA, recognizing the MPC's disjunctive "substantial capacity" standard and the IDRA's strict, cognitive-only "clear and convincing" standard. The distractors fail because they rely on explicit, falsifiable legal misstatements. Option (a) directly contradicts the historical and statutory text of the IDRA by claiming it adopted the volitional prong. Option (c) falsifies the MPC standard by claiming it strictly requires absolute incapacity. Option (d) incorrectly lowers the IDRA's burden of proof to preponderance of the evidence. Option (e) completely mischaracterizes the holding in *Kahler*, which permitted states to abolish the insanity defense but did not ban broader affirmative defenses. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because the IDRA expressly eliminated the volitional prong.
- (c): "falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires" — wrong because the MPC uses the "substantial capacity" standard, not absolute incapacity.
- (d): "Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence." — wrong because the IDRA expressly requires proof by "clear and convincing evidence."
- (e): "The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in Kahler v. Kansas, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea." — wrong because *Kahler* held that the Constitution does not mandate a broader affirmative defense, but it certainly did not prohibit states from maintaining one.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of whether his cognitive appreciation of wrongfulness was merely substantially impaired rather than totally absent.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant must show (1) a "severe" mental disease, (2) inability (not merely substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature and quality or wrongfulness of the act, and (3) proof by clear and convincing evidence; the volitional prong is unavailable.
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate in order to successfully establish an affirmative defense of insanity at trial.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will successfully establish an insanity defense in either federal or state court without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" or "beyond a reasonable doubt" threshold.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior rather than their cognitive intent—is therefore no longer legally available or permissible to assert in any federal or state jurisdiction.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The Model Penal Code requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs. The Insanity Defense Reform Act tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [Model Penal Code-substantial-capacity, volitional-prong, federal-IDRA, burden-of-proof], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on *Kahler v. Kansas*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might choose (a) if they incorrectly recall the legislative history of the Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA). Believing the federal statute modernized the defense by adopting the Model Penal Code trend, they would conclude Dr. Laplace's finding of a total inability to conform conduct perfectly fits the statute. The absolute wording "expressly adopted" acts as a strong lure for a student who has the facts completely backward.
(b) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the law. The Model Penal Code uses a disjunctive test allowing either a lack of substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct; Kant's symptoms clearly satisfy both. In contrast, under the IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17), the volitional prong was eliminated, the mental disease must be "severe," the cognitive impairment must be an inability, and the burden rests on the defendant to prove insanity by clear and convincing evidence. 
(c) Argument-for: A student might defend (c) by confusing the Model Penal Code with the strict, absolute cognitive requirements of M'Naghten. By importing M'Naghten's rigid total-incapacity standard into the MPC context, the student would falsely conclude that the MPC "strictly requires" total absolute incapacity. Consequently, they would deduce that Dr. Laplace's finding of merely "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law.
(d) Argument-for: A student could select (d) by incorrectly generalizing the most common state standard for affirmative defenses—preponderance of the evidence. Believing that burdens of proof for affirmative defenses are structurally uniform across the nation, they would erroneously conclude that the IDRA also utilizes the preponderance standard. Thus, uncontested testimony would easily clear this supposedly uniform bar.
(e) Argument-for: A student could defend (e) if they misunderstood the scope of the holding in *Kahler v. Kansas*. While *Kahler* held that the Constitution does not mandate a volitional or moral-incapacity test, a student might over-read this holding as an affirmative prohibition. In this flawed view, offering an insanity defense that extends beyond mere mens rea would violate constitutional precedent, thereby making the MPC's volitional prong categorically impermissible.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear, legally sound winner because it accurately states the nuanced differences between the MPC and the federal standard post-Hinckley. Every other option features at least one highly confident, explicitly falsifiable legal claim that can be definitively disproven by referencing the plain text of statutes or Supreme Court case law. The distractors are exceptionally well-written, employing strong locking words ("expressly adopted," "strictly requires," "entirely," "fundamentally precludes") that isolate the specific false proposition without relying on vague implications. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the federal statute ... expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because the IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) explicitly eliminated the volitional prong.
- (c): "total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires" — wrong because the MPC § 4.01 explicitly lowered the bar from total incapacity by using the "substantial capacity" standard.
- (d): "the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence" — wrong because the IDRA requires proof of insanity by "clear and convincing evidence."
- (e): "ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea" — wrong because *Kahler* merely held that states are not required by Due Process to go beyond mens rea; it did not preclude or invalidate broader defenses like the MPC.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q20

### Grounding

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Mutina instruction and NGRI civil commitment, Ch 23, Refinement `mutina-instruction` and `ngri-civil-commitment`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

(b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity.
