### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might select this option by reasoning from basic constitutional principles of double jeopardy, which prohibit further punishment following an acquittal. Since an NGRI verdict formally acquits the defendant of criminal responsibility, one might argue that the state must immediately release the defendant and pursue civil commitment through entirely separate, standard procedures under *O'Connor v. Donaldson*. If immediate release is legally required, then an instruction suggesting otherwise would indeed be impermissibly prejudicial and inaccurate.
(b) Argument-for: A student could justify this option by analogizing NGRI commitment to competency restoration limits under *Jackson v. Indiana* or by pointing to general proportionality principles in criminal sentencing. If the fundamental premise of criminal justice caps the deprivation of liberty at the statutory maximum for the charged offense, it logically follows that an NGRI acquittee cannot be held longer than a convicted counterpart. Therefore, a Mutina-style instruction would serve the precise function of advising the jury of this strict statutory cap to prevent unwarranted fears of indefinite hospital stays.
(c) Argument-for: A student might choose this option based on the clinical nature of insanity acquittals, assuming that medical and psychiatric evaluations exclusively dictate the outcome. Because judges are legal professionals lacking medical expertise, one could reasonably conclude that specialized psychiatric security review boards must be the sole arbiters of release in all states recognizing the defense. Under this logic, the Mutina-style instruction is necessary to reassure jurors that public safety decisions post-acquittal are handled strictly by qualified clinical experts.
(d) Argument-for: This is the legally correct answer. Under *Jones v. United States*, NGRI acquittees can be automatically committed to secure psychiatric facilities for periods that often exceed their hypothetical prison sentences, provided they remain mentally ill and dangerous. The Mutina-style instruction correctly explains this reality to jurors, deliberately dispelling the pervasive "revolving door" misconception that an insanity acquittal equates to the defendant walking free and posing an immediate danger to the community.
(e) Argument-for: A student could select this option by mistakenly attributing the Supreme Court's recent insanity jurisprudence in *Kahler v. Kansas* to jury instruction protocols rather than the substantive definition of the defense. If one incorrectly believes *Kahler* broadly struck down procedures that inject public safety concerns into the jury's assessment of mental state, then informing jurors about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines might seem like the exact type of reversible error the Court would prohibit.

Head-to-head:
(d) correctly states the empirical reality of NGRI commitments (often longer than standard sentences) and the legal purpose of a Mutina-style instruction (dispelling the misconception of immediate release). (a) fails because *Jones v. United States* explicitly permits the automatic commitment of NGRI acquittees without violating Double Jeopardy. (b) fails because *Jones* also established that NGRI commitments are not strictly capped at the statutory maximum for the underlying offense. (c) is easily falsified by the absolute phrase "In every jurisdiction"; many jurisdictions leave final release authority with judges, not psychiatric security review boards. (e) fails because *Kahler v. Kansas* dealt with the substantive constitutionality of abolishing the moral-incapacity prong of the insanity defense, not the procedural constitutionality of a Mutina-style jury instruction.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal" — wrong because *Jones v. United States* establishes that automatic civil commitment after an NGRI verdict is constitutional.
- (b): "Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence... regardless of clinical status" — wrong because the Supreme Court in *Jones v. United States* held that NGRI acquittees can be confined longer than their hypothetical maximum prison sentences as long as clinical criteria are met.
- (c): "In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board... has exclusive authority" — wrong because the vast majority of jurisdictions place final release authority in the hands of the trial judge, not an exclusive review board.
- (e): "The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in Kahler v. Kansas" — wrong because *Kahler* upheld Kansas's approach to the substantive insanity defense (abolishing the moral-wrongness test) and did not hold instructions on sentencing/commitment consequences unconstitutional.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the principles of due process, an acquittal typically restores a defendant's presumption of liberty. One could argue that an NGRI acquittee must therefore be immediately released until separate, formal civil commitment proceedings are successfully concluded under *O'Connor v. Donaldson* standards. Because the trial court cannot guarantee the outcome of a separate civil proceeding, a *Mutina*-style instruction is arguably prejudicial because it falsely promises a loss of liberty that the court has no constitutional power to impose automatically. Thus, (a) presents a plausible argument about the tension between an acquittal and involuntary confinement.

(b) Argument-for: Due process principles often require that the nature and duration of commitment bear a reasonable relation to its purpose. Some jurisdictions have addressed equal protection concerns by tying NGRI commitments to the maximum sentence the defendant would have served if convicted, aiming to treat acquittees and convicts similarly. If a jurisdiction applied this strict maximum, a *Mutina*-style instruction would need to inform the jury of the cap so they do not wrongly assume the commitment is permanent. Option (b) articulates a recognizable theoretical approach to proportionality in commitment duration.

(c) Argument-for: The creation of a Psychiatric Security Review Board (PSRB) is a well-regarded modern approach to handling NGRI acquittees, pioneered by states like Oregon. The core premise of the PSRB model is to transfer release authority from the judiciary to a panel of experts who are better equipped to evaluate clinical dangerousness. To completely insulate decisions from political pressure, courts would logically be barred from overriding the board. Option (c) could be defended by emphasizing the PSRB's specialized release authority and connecting it to the *Mutina* instruction's goal of reassuring the jury.

(d) Argument-for: Option (d) correctly states the empirical reality and legal consequence of an NGRI verdict, which in the vast majority of jurisdictions results in automatic or near-automatic commitment (*Jones v. United States*). Because the duration of commitment is tied to clinical recovery (dangerousness and mental illness) rather than a penal sentence, NGRI acquittees frequently spend longer confined than they would have in prison. To counteract the pervasive "insanity defense myth" that acquittees go free immediately, jurisdictions utilize the *Mutina*-style instruction to truthfully inform the jury about the commitment consequence, preventing wrongful convictions based on unfounded public-safety fears.

(e) Argument-for: The U.S. Supreme Court's jurisprudence generally dictates that juries are not supposed to be instructed on the sentencing or post-verdict consequences of their decisions (*Shannon v. United States*). One could argue that *Kahler v. Kansas*, which addressed the constitutional limits of the insanity defense, implicitly reinforced the idea that providing jurors with detailed commitment timelines invites them to decide criminal responsibility on impermissible public safety grounds. Thus, option (e) provides a plausible constitutional argument that *Mutina*-style instructions improperly conflate the jury's fact-finding role with dispositional consequences.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is legally and empirically accurate, capturing the holding of *Jones v. United States* (permitting automatic commitment and indefinite duration tied to clinical status) and the specific corrective purpose of the *Mutina* instruction. All distractors contain lock-solid, falsifiable legal or historical errors. Option (a) incorrectly equates an NGRI verdict with a general acquittal, ignoring *Jones*. Option (b) falsely fabricates a universal, strict statutory cap based on maximum sentences. Option (c) uses absolute language ("In every jurisdiction... categorically barred") to describe the PSRB model, which is actually only used in a small minority of states. Option (e) completely fabricates a holding for *Kahler v. Kansas*, which dealt with the abolition of the moral-incapacity prong of the insanity defense, not jury instructions.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal" — wrong because *Jones v. United States* constitutionally permits automatic commitment for NGRI acquittees.
- (b): "Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence... must be released regardless of clinical status" — wrong because NGRI commitment duration is fundamentally tied to the acquittee's clinical status (mental illness and dangerousness), not a strict penal maximum.
- (c): "In every jurisdiction... a psychiatric security review board... has exclusive authority to order release" — wrong because only a minority of jurisdictions use PSRBs; most retain judicial release authority.
- (e): "The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in Kahler v. Kansas" — wrong because *Kahler* did not address jury instructions on civil commitment consequences.

Recommended fix: none
